الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه علي زين العابدين ثم ابنه محمد الباقر، ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم، ثم ابنه علي الرضا، ثم ابنه محمد الجواد ثم ابنه علي الزكي ثم ابنه الحسن العسكري ثم ابنه محمد بن القائم المنتظر المهدي ويدعون أنه ثبت بالتواتر نص كل من السابقين على من بعده، ويروون عن النبي أنه قال للحسين رضي الله عنه ابني هذا، إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة تاسعهم قائمهم. ويتمسكون تارة بأنه يجب في الإمام العصمة، والأفضلية ولا يوجدان فيمن سواهم. والعاقل يتعجب من هذه الروايات والمتواترات التي لا أثر لها في القرون السابقة من أسلافهم، ولا رواية عن العترة الطاهرة ومن يوثق بهم من الرواة المحدثين. وأنه كيف يأتي من زيد بن علي رضي الله عنه مع جلالة قدره دعوى لخلافة وكيف لم تبلغه هذه المتواترات بعد مائة وقد بلغت آحاد الروافض بعد سبعمائة ثم لسائر فرق الشيعة في باب الإمامة اختلافات لا تحصى ذكر الإمام في المحصل نبذاً منها.
And Muhammad ibn Maslama, except that they asked to be excused from fighting against the people of the qibla due to the hadiths they narrated on this meaning. In summary, his caliphate was established by the pledge of allegiance and the agreement of the people of authority and influence. Hadiths have indicated it, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.' And his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You will fight the breakers of the pledge, the deviators, and the wrongdoers.' And his saying (peace be upon him) to Ammar: 'The rebellious group will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Among the theologians are those who claim consensus on his caliphate because it was established by the consensus of the time of the shura, that the caliphate was for Uthman or Ali. It is a consensus that had it not been for Uthman, it would have been for Ali, so when Uthman left the scene by being killed, it remained for Ali by consensus. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'No heed is paid to the statement of those who say there is no consensus on the imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him); for the imamate was not denied to him; rather, the fitnas arose for other reasons.' He said: 'As for the Shiites, they claim that the Imam after the Prophet is Ali (may Allah ennoble his face), then al-Hasan, then al-Husayn, then Ali Zayn al-Abidin, then Muhammad al-Baqir, then Ja'far al-Sadiq, then Musa al-Kazim, then Ali al-Rida, then Muhammad al-Jawad, then Ali al-Zaki, then al-Hasan al-Askari, then Muhammad al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and that the designation of each by the one before him is mutawatir, and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Husayn: "This son of mine is an Imam, son of an Imam, brother of an Imam, father of nine Imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im." And we can only express astonishment.' The Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is Ali, then his son al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son Ali Zayn al-Abidin, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kazim, then his son Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Jawad, then his son Ali al-Zaki, then his son al-Hasan al-Askari, then his son Muhammad ibn al-Qa'im al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and they claim that it is established by tawatur that each of the previous ones designated the one after him, and they narrate from the Prophet that he said to al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him): 'This son of mine is an Imam, son of an Imam, brother of an Imam, father of nine Imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im.' They also sometimes argue that the Imam must be infallible and superior, and these qualities are not found in others. The rational person is astonished at these narrations and tawatur which have no trace in the earlier centuries of their forefathers, nor any narration from the pure Ahl al-Bayt and from trustworthy narrators and hadith transmitters. And how is it that Zayd ibn Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), despite his great status, claimed the caliphate? And why did these tawatur not reach him after a hundred years, whereas they reached common Rafidis after seven hundred? Then for the other sects of the Shi'ah concerning the matter of imamate, there are countless differences; the Imam (the author) has mentioned a sample of them in al-Muhassal.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid deals with the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and refutes the Twelver Shiite doctrine of imamate. The author begins by mentioning a group (Muhammad ibn Maslama and others) who excused themselves from fighting against fellow Muslims in battles like Jamal and Siffin, citing hadiths. Then he affirms that Ali's caliphate was established by consensus of the people of authority. He cites several prophetic hadiths: the caliphate lasting thirty years (which includes Ali), the prediction that Ali would fight the three groups (the breakers of pledges, the deviators, and the oppressors), and the hadith about Ammar being killed by the rebellious group. The author notes that some theologians claim consensus on Ali's caliphate because the shura (consultative council) was either for Uthman or Ali, implying that when Uthman died, Ali became the consensus choice. He then quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) dismissing the objection that there is no consensus on Ali's imamate, since the imamate was never denied to him; the civil wars (fitnas) were due to other reasons. Next, the author presents the Shiite (specifically Imami/Ithna Ashari) claim: that the Imam after the Prophet is Ali, followed by a list of twelve Imams, and that each Imam was explicitly designated (nass) by his predecessor in a mutawatir manner. They also claim a hadith from the Prophet to al-Husayn that his descendants would be nine Imams. The author dismisses this with astonishment. He then repeats the Shiite claim in more detail, noting their additional argument that the Imam must be infallible (ma'sum) and superior (afdal), and these qualities are not found in others. The author refutes these claims by pointing out that these alleged mutawatir traditions have no trace in earlier centuries of their own predecessors (the Salaf), and are not narrated from the pure Ahl al-Bayt or reliable hadith transmitters. He asks: how did Zayd ibn Ali, a respected figure from the Prophet's family, claim the caliphate if these designations were known? Why did these mutawatir reports not reach him after a hundred years, but only reached common Rafidis after seven hundred? This shows the weakness of the Shiite claim. Finally, the author notes that there are countless differences among Shiite sects regarding imamate, and he refers to a sample in al-Muhassal (by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi?).

إجماع
بيعة

According to the author, what does the hadith 'The caliphate after me is thirty years' indicate?

C) That the caliphate ended after thirty years
D) That Uthman's caliphate was the longest

Key Takeaway
Ali's caliphate was validated by both community consensus and prophetic hadiths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study historical conflicts among the Companions, do you find yourself leaning toward a particular side based on modern loyalties or inherited narratives? Today, can you commit to seeking the truth from primary sources like the hadiths and consensus arguments presented here, even if it challenges your assumptions?
?
The author quotes Imam al-Haramayn saying the fitnas arose for 'other reasons.' Could it be that in your own relationships or community conflicts, you also misattribute the cause? Will you pause to examine whether the real issue is something else—perhaps personal ambition or misunderstanding—rather than a disagreement on fundamentals?
?
The Prophet's hadiths about Ali and Ammar gave clear signs about who was on the right side. Do you have the courage to stand with what the evidence shows, even if the majority of people around you take a different stance? Are you willing to be like Ammar, fighting under the banner of truth even at the cost of your life?

The Twelver Shiite Doctrine of Imamate
Author presents the Twelver Shiite doctrine of imamate with their list of Imams and arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Twelver Shiite claim regarding the twelve Imams, the principle of nass (explicit designation), and their arguments including the hadith about nine Imams and the necessity of infallibility.

Core Idea
Shiites claim a chain of twelve infallible Imams, each explicitly designated by the previous one.

The author now presents the views of the Shiites (specifically the Imamiyyah) on imamate. They assert that after the Prophet, the rightful Imam is Ali, followed by eleven of his descendants, and that the designation of each Imam is established by tawatur. They also cite a prophetic hadith about al-Husayn being the father of nine Imams and argue that the Imam must be infallible and superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shiite claim
Read the Arabic passage that presents the Shiite doctrine. Note the list of names from Ali to Muhammad al-Mahdi.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The Shiites rely on two claims: that each Imam designated his successor (nass) in a mutawatir manner, and that the Prophet said to al-Husayn that his descendants would be nine Imams. They also argue from infallibility and superiority.
3
Consider the implications
Think about what this doctrine means for the legitimacy of other caliphs and the early community.
This doctrine is a core difference between Sunni and Shia Islam.

صلى الله عليه وسلم قال للحسين ابني هذا إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة، تاسعهم قائمهم. ونحن لا نزيد على التعجب. أن الإمامية يزعمون أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم علي، ثم ابنه الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه علي زين العابدين ثم ابنه محمد الباقر، ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم، ثم ابنه علي الرضا، ثم ابنه محمد الجواد ثم ابنه علي الزكي ثم ابنه الحسن العسكري ثم ابنه محمد بن القائم المنتظر المهدي ويدعون أنه ثبت بالتواتر نص كل من السابقين على من بعده، ويروون عن النبي أنه قال للحسين رضي الله عنه ابني هذا، إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة تاسعهم قائمهم. ويتمسكون تارة بأنه يجب في الإمام العصمة، والأفضلية ولا يوجدان فيمن سواهم. والعاقل يتعجب من هذه الروايات والمتواترات التي لا أثر لها في القرون السابقة من أسلافهم، ولا رواية عن العترة الطاهرة ومن يوثق بهم من الرواة المحدثين. وأنه كيف يأتي من زيد بن علي رضي الله عنه مع جلالة قدره دعوى لخلافة وكيف لم تبلغه هذه المتواترات بعد مائة وقد بلغت آحاد الروافض بعد سبعمائة ثم لسائر فرق الشيعة في باب الإمامة اختلافات لا تحصى ذكر الإمام في المحصل نبذاً منها.
And Muhammad ibn Maslama, except that they asked to be excused from fighting against the people of the qibla due to the hadiths they narrated on this meaning. In summary, his caliphate was established by the pledge of allegiance and the agreement of the people of authority and influence. Hadiths have indicated it, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.' And his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You will fight the breakers of the pledge, the deviators, and the wrongdoers.' And his saying (peace be upon him) to Ammar: 'The rebellious group will kill you.' And he was killed on the day of Siffin under the banner of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Among the theologians are those who claim consensus on his caliphate because it was established by the consensus of the time of the shura, that the caliphate was for Uthman or Ali. It is a consensus that had it not been for Uthman, it would have been for Ali, so when Uthman left the scene by being killed, it remained for Ali by consensus. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'No heed is paid to the statement of those who say there is no consensus on the imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him); for the imamate was not denied to him; rather, the fitnas arose for other reasons.' He said: 'As for the Shiites, they claim that the Imam after the Prophet is Ali (may Allah ennoble his face), then al-Hasan, then al-Husayn, then Ali Zayn al-Abidin, then Muhammad al-Baqir, then Ja'far al-Sadiq, then Musa al-Kazim, then Ali al-Rida, then Muhammad al-Jawad, then Ali al-Zaki, then al-Hasan al-Askari, then Muhammad al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and that the designation of each by the one before him is mutawatir, and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to al-Husayn: "This son of mine is an Imam, son of an Imam, brother of an Imam, father of nine Imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im." And we can only express astonishment.' The Imamiyya claim that the true Imam after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is Ali, then his son al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son Ali Zayn al-Abidin, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kazim, then his son Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Jawad, then his son Ali al-Zaki, then his son al-Hasan al-Askari, then his son Muhammad ibn al-Qa'im al-Muntazar al-Mahdi, and they claim that it is established by tawatur that each of the previous ones designated the one after him, and they narrate from the Prophet that he said to al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him): 'This son of mine is an Imam, son of an Imam, brother of an Imam, father of nine Imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im.' They also sometimes argue that the Imam must be infallible and superior, and these qualities are not found in others. The rational person is astonished at these narrations and tawatur which have no trace in the earlier centuries of their forefathers, nor any narration from the pure Ahl al-Bayt and from trustworthy narrators and hadith transmitters. And how is it that Zayd ibn Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), despite his great status, claimed the caliphate? And why did these tawatur not reach him after a hundred years, whereas they reached common Rafidis after seven hundred? Then for the other sects of the Shi'ah concerning the matter of imamate, there are countless differences; the Imam (the author) has mentioned a sample of them in al-Muhassal.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid deals with the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and refutes the Twelver Shiite doctrine of imamate. The author begins by mentioning a group (Muhammad ibn Maslama and others) who excused themselves from fighting against fellow Muslims in battles like Jamal and Siffin, citing hadiths. Then he affirms that Ali's caliphate was established by consensus of the people of authority. He cites several prophetic hadiths: the caliphate lasting thirty years (which includes Ali), the prediction that Ali would fight the three groups (the breakers of pledges, the deviators, and the oppressors), and the hadith about Ammar being killed by the rebellious group. The author notes that some theologians claim consensus on Ali's caliphate because the shura (consultative council) was either for Uthman or Ali, implying that when Uthman died, Ali became the consensus choice. He then quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) dismissing the objection that there is no consensus on Ali's imamate, since the imamate was never denied to him; the civil wars (fitnas) were due to other reasons. Next, the author presents the Shiite (specifically Imami/Ithna Ashari) claim: that the Imam after the Prophet is Ali, followed by a list of twelve Imams, and that each Imam was explicitly designated (nass) by his predecessor in a mutawatir manner. They also claim a hadith from the Prophet to al-Husayn that his descendants would be nine Imams. The author dismisses this with astonishment. He then repeats the Shiite claim in more detail, noting their additional argument that the Imam must be infallible (ma'sum) and superior (afdal), and these qualities are not found in others. The author refutes these claims by pointing out that these alleged mutawatir traditions have no trace in earlier centuries of their own predecessors (the Salaf), and are not narrated from the pure Ahl al-Bayt or reliable hadith transmitters. He asks: how did Zayd ibn Ali, a respected figure from the Prophet's family, claim the caliphate if these designations were known? Why did these mutawatir reports not reach him after a hundred years, but only reached common Rafidis after seven hundred? This shows the weakness of the Shiite claim. Finally, the author notes that there are countless differences among Shiite sects regarding imamate, and he refers to a sample in al-Muhassal (by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi?).

Key Terms
إمامة
نص
تواتر
عصمة
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many Imams do the Twelver Shiites believe exist after the Prophet according to the passage?
A) 12
B) 11
C) 9
D) 14

Key Takeaway
Twelver Shiites assert a continuous chain of twelve divinely designated Imams from the Prophet's family, supported by arguments of designations and infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the claim that there is a continuous chain of divinely guided leaders from the Prophet's family, do you feel a sense of loss that you are not connected to them? Or do you feel satisfied that your understanding of leadership is different? What evidence would it take for you to change your position?
?
If you were to apply the same standard of proof (mass transmission, explicit designation) to your own religious beliefs, would they hold up? Are there areas where you accept tradition without scrutinizing the chain of transmission? Be honest: what is one belief you hold that you have never verified from primary sources?
?
The Twelvers assert that their Imams are infallible. In your own life, do you treat any religious figure or scholar as if they were infallible? How might that affect your willingness to accept correction? Will you commit to questioning any such assumption today?

Sects and Disagreements

Refutation of the Shiite Imamate Claim
Author refutes the Shiite Imamate claim using historical evidence and logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the Twelver Shiite Imamate, particularly the argument from historical silence and the example of Zayd ibn Ali.

Core Idea
The Shiite claims of tawatur are historically unfounded and were unknown to early Imams like Zayd ibn Ali.

In this passage, the author expresses astonishment at the Shiite claims and provides a powerful refutation. He points out that these alleged mutawatir traditions have no trace in the early centuries, were not transmitted by reliable narrators from the Ahl al-Bayt, and were unknown to Zayd ibn Ali—a respected descendant who claimed the caliphate only a century after the Prophet. This shows the fabricated nature of the traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage that refutes the Shiite claims. Note the author's tone and the specific arguments.
2
Analyze the argument from historical silence
The author notes that these mutawatir claims have no trace in earlier centuries from the Salaf (forefathers) and no reliable chain of transmission.
3
Examine the Zayd ibn Ali example
The author asks: if the designations were mutawatir, why was Zayd ibn Ali—a prominent descendant of al-Husayn—unaware of them when he claimed the imamate? This is a strong historical counterargument.
4
Consider the diversity of Shiite sects
The author notes that there are countless differences among Shiite sects on imamate, which casts doubt on any one claim.

Source Text

ومحمد بن مسلمة إلا أنهم استعفوا عن القتال مع أهل القبلة لما رووا في هذا المعنى من الأحاديث. وبالجملة انعقدت خلافته بالبيعة واتفاق أهل الحل والعقد. وقد دلت عليه أحاديث كقوله عليه السلام الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة وقوله عليه السلام لعلي رضي الله تعالى عنه إنك تقاتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين. وقوله عليه السلام لعمار تقتلك الفئة الباغية. وقد قتل يوم صفين تحت راية علي رضي الله تعالى عنه. ومن المتكلمين من يدعي الإجماع على خلافته لأنه انعقد لإجماع زمان الشورى. على أن الخلافة لعثمان أو علي. وهو إجماع على أنه لولا عثمان فهي لعلي فحين خرج عثمان من البين بالقتل، بقي لعلي بالإجماع. قال إمام الحرمين لا اكتراث بقول من قال لا إجماع على إمامة علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فإن الإمامة لم تجحد له، وإنما هاجت الفتن لأمور أخر. قال وأما الشيعة فيزعمون أن الإمام بعد النبي علي كرم الله وجهه ثم الحسن، ثم الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه علي زين العابدين ثم ابنه محمد الباقر، ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم، ثم ابنه علي الرضا، ثم ابنه محمد الجواد ثم ابنه علي الزكي ثم ابنه الحسن العسكري ثم ابنه محمد بن القائم المنتظر المهدي ويدعون أنه ثبت بالتواتر نص كل من السابقين على من بعده، ويروون عن النبي أنه قال للحسين رضي الله عنه ابني هذا، إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة تاسعهم قائمهم. ويتمسكون تارة How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you discovered that a deeply held belief of yours had no support from the earliest authorities of your tradition, would you have the courage to re-examine it—even if it meant distancing yourself from your community?
?
Zayd ibn Ali, a direct descendant of the Prophet, acted on what he knew to be true without any alleged designation. Are your own convictions based on what you have verified, or on what you have inherited uncritically?
?
The author points to internal disagreement among Shiites as evidence against a single divine appointment. When you see divisions among Muslims on a matter, do you use that to seek the truth, or to justify clinging to your own group's position?

The Sunni Position and Quranic Evidence for Superiority
The author states the Sunni and opposing views, then gives a general argument and the first detailed proof from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the order of the caliphate regarding superiority and the Quranic proof from the verse about Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The order of the caliphate reflects the order of superiority, supported by the Quran and Hadith.

The issue of superiority among the Companions is a key point of divergence between Sunni and Shiite/Mu'tazilite theology. In this passage, the author begins by stating the Sunni consensus and then presents the first piece of detailed evidence: a Quranic verse revealed about Abu Bakr. Let's explore this foundational claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Read the opening lines to see what the Sunni view is and how it differs from the Shiite and Mu'tazilite view.
2
Understand the general argument
The author gives a general argument from consensus: most scholars agree on this order, so there must be a proof for it.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse
Examine the verse 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it' and its interpretation that it refers to Abu Bakr, implying his superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المبحث السادس الأفضلية عندنا بترتيب الخلافة مع تردد فيما بين عثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما. وعند الشيعة وجمهور المعتزلة الأفضل علي. لنا إجمالاً أن اتفاق أكثر العلماء على ذلك يقضي بوجود دليل لهم. وتفصيلا قوله تعالى وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتي ماله يتزكى نزلت في أبي بكر. والأتقى أكرم وأفضل. وقوله عليه السلام اقتدوا باللذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر فقد أمر على بالاقتداء بهما. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم هما سيدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر.
He said: The sixth chapter: Superiority (al-afdaliyya). According to us (Ahl al-Sunnah), it is according to the order of the caliphate, with hesitation between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both). According to the Shiites and the majority of the Mu'tazilites, Ali is superior. Our proof in general is that the agreement of most scholars on this necessitates the existence of a proof for them. And in detail: His (Exalted) saying: 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it, the one who gives his wealth to purify himself' – it was revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'the most righteous' (al-atqa) is more honorable and superior. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar'; thus he commanded Ali to follow them. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'They are the masters of the old men of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his saying (peace be upon him): 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar.'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid is classified as a proof for the Sunni position on the superiority (afdaliyya) of the caliphs. The author begins by stating the Sunni view: the order of the caliphate (Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali) indicates superiority, although there is a debate between Uthman and Ali (some scholars prefer Uthman, others Ali). He then notes the opposing views: the Shiites and most Mu'tazilites hold Ali to be superior. The author presents a general argument (ijmalan): the agreement of the majority of scholars on this order implies that they have proof, so we accept it. He then provides detailed evidence (tafsilan): first, a Quranic verse, 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it...' which was revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'the most righteous' indicates honor and superiority. Second, the hadith 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar' – this is a command to all, including Ali, to follow them, showing their superiority. Third, the hadith that they are the masters of the old men of Paradise, indicating their rank. Fourth, the hadith 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar' – explicitly stating their superiority in order.

Key Terms
أفضلية
خلافة
أتقى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view on afdaliyya is the same as the Shiite view.
Correction: The author distinguishes them but both hold Ali as superior.

Checkpoint
What is the general argument (ijmalan) presented by the author?
A) The Quranic verse proves Abu Bakr's superiority.
B) The consensus of most scholars implies a valid proof exists.
C) Ali's superiority is affirmed by hadith.
D) The order of the caliphate is coincidental.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on superiority is based on the order of the caliphate, supported by a general argument from consensus and a specific Quranic verse indicating Abu Bakr's superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the order of the caliphate—Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali—does your heart truly submit to this ranking as a reflection of divine wisdom, or do you find yourself influenced by later historical narratives that question it? What will you do to align your conviction with the Quranic evidence presented here?
?
The verse about Abu Bakr was revealed to highlight his unique righteousness (taqwa). Does your own life display a similar, persistent pursuit of righteousness, or have you become complacent? Identify one specific area where you can increase your taqwa starting today, inspired by Abu Bakr's example.
?
This lesson uses the consensus of scholars as a proof. Do you trust the collective understanding of the early Muslim community (salaf) more than your own individual reasoning? Examine your heart: are there any articles of faith you hold that contradict the mainstream Sunni position? If so, what will you do to seek the truth and follow the way of Ahl al-Sunnah?

Prophetic Hadiths on the Superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar
The author provides three hadiths as detailed evidence for the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three prophetic hadiths that serve as detailed evidence for the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar over Ali.

Core Idea
Hadiths command following Abu Bakr and Umar and declare them the best of the community.

After presenting the Quranic evidence, the author adds three hadiths that explicitly establish the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar. These hadiths are used by Sunni scholars to argue that the order of the caliphate is not merely political but reflects spiritual rank. Let's examine each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the first hadith
The hadith 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar' commands Ali and all believers to follow them, implying their superiority.
2
Read the second hadith
The hadith 'They are the masters of the old men of Paradise' assigns them a unique rank in Paradise.
3
Read the third hadith
The hadith 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar' explicitly ranks them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المبحث السادس الأفضلية عندنا بترتيب الخلافة مع تردد فيما بين عثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما. وعند الشيعة وجمهور المعتزلة الأفضل علي. لنا إجمالاً أن اتفاق أكثر العلماء على ذلك يقضي بوجود دليل لهم. وتفصيلا قوله تعالى وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتي ماله يتزكى نزلت في أبي بكر. والأتقى أكرم وأفضل. وقوله عليه السلام اقتدوا باللذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر فقد أمر على بالاقتداء بهما. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم هما سيدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر.
He said: The sixth chapter: Superiority (al-afdaliyya). According to us (Ahl al-Sunnah), it is according to the order of the caliphate, with hesitation between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both). According to the Shiites and the majority of the Mu'tazilites, Ali is superior. Our proof in general is that the agreement of most scholars on this necessitates the existence of a proof for them. And in detail: His (Exalted) saying: 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it, the one who gives his wealth to purify himself' – it was revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'the most righteous' (al-atqa) is more honorable and superior. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar'; thus he commanded Ali to follow them. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'They are the masters of the old men of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his saying (peace be upon him): 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar.'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid is classified as a proof for the Sunni position on the superiority (afdaliyya) of the caliphs. The author begins by stating the Sunni view: the order of the caliphate (Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali) indicates superiority, although there is a debate between Uthman and Ali (some scholars prefer Uthman, others Ali). He then notes the opposing views: the Shiites and most Mu'tazilites hold Ali to be superior. The author presents a general argument (ijmalan): the agreement of the majority of scholars on this order implies that they have proof, so we accept it. He then provides detailed evidence (tafsilan): first, a Quranic verse, 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it...' which was revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'the most righteous' indicates honor and superiority. Second, the hadith 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar' – this is a command to all, including Ali, to follow them, showing their superiority. Third, the hadith that they are the masters of the old men of Paradise, indicating their rank. Fourth, the hadith 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar' – explicitly stating their superiority in order.

Key Terms
اقتداء
سيدا
خير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ali
B) Umar
C) Uthman
D) The Prophet

Key Takeaway
Three prophetic hadiths explicitly command following Abu Bakr and Umar and declare them the best of the community, forming strong evidence for their superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept in your heart that Abu Bakr and Umar are the best of this community after the Prophet? If not, what is holding you back from submitting to these clear hadiths? What would it cost you to fully embrace this teaching?
?
The Prophet commanded even Ali, his cousin and son-in-law, to follow Abu Bakr and Umar. Is there any pride in your life that prevents you from following those whom the Prophet commanded you to follow? Will you examine your heart for any trace of preference over the Prophet's command?
?
If you were to rank the companions in your own mind, would your ranking align with the explicit words of the Prophet: 'The best of my community is Abu Bakr, then Umar'? What adjustment do you need to make in your heart to bring it in line with this truth?

Refutation of the Shi'a Tawatur Claim for the Twelve Imams
The author enumerates the twelve imams and refutes their alleged tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key Sunni critique of the Shi'a claim that the imamate of the twelve imams is established by tawatur (widely transmitted reports).

Core Idea
The Shi'a tawatur claim for the twelve imams has no historical trace in early centuries.

The author directly challenges the Shi'a doctrine of the twelve imams and their assertion that each imam was designated by the previous one through tawatur. He lists the twelve imams as accepted by the Twelver Shi'a, then presents the alleged prophetic hadith. But he immediately expresses astonishment that these purportedly mass-transmitted reports left no record among early trustworthy narrators. This passage lays out the author's historical and logical counter-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a claim
Read the passage and identify the list of twelve imams and the hadith they cite from the Prophet.
Notice the names and the chain of succession.
2
Understand the author's initial rebuttal
The author expresses astonishment that these reports have no trace in earlier centuries and are not transmitted by reliable narrators from the Prophet's family.
This attacks the tawatur claim directly.
3
Analyze the Zayd ibn Ali example
Consider why Zayd's claim to the caliphate, despite his proximity to the prophetic era, undermines the Shi'a claim that these reports were widespread early on.
This is a powerful historical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه علي زين العابدين ثم ابنه محمد الباقر، ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم، ثم ابنه علي الرضا، ثم ابنه محمد الجواد ثم ابنه علي الزكي ثم ابنه الحسن العسكري ثم ابنه محمد بن القائم المنتظر المهدي ويدعون أنه ثبت بالتواتر نص كل من السابقين على من بعده، ويروون عن النبي أنه قال للحسين رضي الله عنه : ابني هذا، إمام ابن إمام أخو إمام أبو أئمة تسعة تاسعهم قائمهم. ويتمسكون تارة بأنه يجب في الإمام العصمة ،والأفضلية ولا يوجدان فيمن سواهم . والعاقل يتعجب من هذه الروايات والمتواترات التي لا أثر لها في القرون السابقة من أسلافهم ، ولا رواية عن العترة الطاهرة ومن يوثق بهم من الرواة المحدثين. وأنه كيف يأتي من زيد بن علي (رضي الله عنه) مع جلالة قدره دعوى لخلافة ؟ وكيف لم تبلغه هذه المتواترات بعد مائة وقد بلغت آحاد الروافض بعد سبعمائة؟ ثم لسائر فرق الشيعة في باب الإمامة اختلافات لا تحصى ذكر الإمام في المحصل نبذاً منها .
Al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son Ali Zayn al-Abidin, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kazim, then his son Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Jawad, then his son Ali al-Zaki, then his son al-Hasan al-Askari, then his son Muhammad ibn al-Qa'im al-Muntazar al-Mahdi. And they claim that it is established by tawatur (widely transmitted report) that each of the predecessors designated the one after him, and they narrate from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said to al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him): 'This son of mine is an imam, son of an imam, brother of an imam, father of nine imams, the ninth of whom is their Qa'im.' And they sometimes cling to the argument that the imam must possess 'isma (infallibility) and afdaliyya (superior excellence), and these are not found in others. But the intelligent person is astonished at these reports and tawatur claims which have no trace in the previous centuries of their ancestors, nor any narration from the pure family (al-'itra al-tahira) and from trustworthy hadith transmitters. And how is it that Zayd ibn Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), despite his great status, claimed the caliphate? How did these tawatur reports not reach him after one hundred years, while they reached ordinary Rafidis after seven hundred? Then, for the remaining sects of the Shi'a concerning the matter of imama, there are countless differences which the Imam mentioned in al-Muhassal as examples.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shi'a claim that the Imamate of the twelve imams is established by tawatur. The author first lists the Shi'a line of twelve imams and their alleged hadith from the Prophet. He then notes their argument that the imam must be infallible and superior, which they claim only applies to their imams. The author responds with astonishment, pointing out that these purportedly widespread reports have no trace in earlier centuries, nor are they transmitted by reliable narrators from the Prophet's family. He uses the example of Zayd ibn Ali, a revered figure who claimed the caliphate despite living closer to the time of the Prophet, yet these tawatur reports allegedly reached later Rafidis but not him. The passage concludes by noting the vast disagreements among Shi'a sects on imama, citing al-Muhassal as a source. The overall approach is critical, questioning the historical veracity of the Shi'a narrative.

عصمة

إمامة
روافض
عترة طاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadiths about the twelve imams are mutawatir and historically verified.
Correction: These reports have no trace in early centuries and are not transmitted by reliable narrators from the Prophet's family.

Checkpoint

B) Because these reports have no trace in earlier centuries and are not transmitted by reliable narrators.
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly rejects the concept of twelve imams.
D) Because all Shi'a sects agree on the same list.

Key Takeaway
The claim that the imamate of the twelve imams is established by tawatur is historically unsubstantiated, as evidenced by the absence of such reports in early centuries and the case of Zayd ibn Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a religious belief or story simply because it came from a group you revere, without honestly examining whether it has early and reliable evidence? What would it cost you to submit your convictions to historical truth?
?
When you encounter a claim about Islam that seems convenient or supportive of your existing views, do you instinctively accept it, or do you pause to ask: 'Where is this in the early sources?' What might that pause reveal about your own intellectual integrity?
?
Zayd ibn Ali, a direct descendant of the Prophet, acted in a way that contradicted the Shi'a imamate doctrine. Are you willing to let the actions of a historically respected figure challenge your current understanding—even if it means rethinking something you hold dear? What would it take for you to change a deeply rooted belief if the evidence demanded it?

The Sunni Position on Superiority of the Caliphs
Sanusi states the Sunni position and summary proof on afdaliyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni view on the order of caliphal superiority and the summary proof from scholarly consensus.

Core Idea
The order of caliphs is the order of their superiority.

The author begins by stating the Sunni position on the superiority of the caliphs, which follows the historical order of the caliphate, with a minor hesitation between Uthman and Ali. He then presents a summary proof based on the consensus of scholars, which indicates that there must be evidence for this ranking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sunni position
Read the first sentence to understand that the order of superiority is the order of the caliphate, with a slight hesitation between Uthman and Ali.
Note that this is the baseline Sunni view.
2
Contrast with other groups
Notice that the Shi'a and most Mu'tazila hold Ali as superior. This sets up the debate.
3
Understand the summary proof
The author argues that the consensus of most scholars on this ranking indicates that there must be evidence for it. Think about why consensus is a proof.
This is not the detailed proof, but a preliminary argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث السادس - الله الأفضلية) عندنا بترتيب الخلافة مع تردد فيما بين عثمان وعلي (رضي عنهما). وعند الشيعة وجمهور المعتزلة الأفضل علي. لنا إجمالاً أن اتفاق أكثر العلماء على ذلك يقضي بوجود دليل لهم. وتفصيلا قوله تعالى وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتي ماله يتزكى (١) . نزلت في أبي بكر . والأتقى أكرم وأفضل. وقوله (عليه السلام): اقتدوا باللذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر (۲) فقد أمر على بالاقتداء بهما . وقوله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) هما سیدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين . وقوله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر . وقوله (عليه السلام ما طلعت الشمس ولا غربت بعد النبيين والمرسلين على أحد أفضل من أبي بكر. وفيها كثرة . وقال عليه السلام) : لو كان من بعدي نبي لكان عمر. وقال: «الا استحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء» (۱) وقد ثبت القول بهذا عن علي وابن عمر وابن الحنفية ودل عليه ما تواتر من آثارهم وأخبارهم ومساعيهم في الإسلام، ومن تألف القلوب وتتابع الفتوح، وقهر أهل الردة وكسر فارس والروم ومن فتح الشرق، وقمع دولة العجم وترتيب الأمور، وإفاضته العدل، وتقوية الضعفاء، ومن فتح البلاد وإعلاء كلمة الله وجمع الناس على مصحف واحد، وتجهيز الجيوش، وإنفاق الأموال في نصرة الدين، ونحو ذلك)
He said: The sixth section — on divine superiority (al-afdaliyya). According to us (the Sunnis), [the order is] according to the sequence of the caliphate, with hesitation between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). According to the Shi'a and the majority of the Mu'tazila, Ali is superior (al-afdal). Our proof, in summary, is that the agreement of most scholars on that indicates the existence of a proof for them. And in detail, His saying (Exalted): 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself' (92:17-18). This was revealed about Abu Bakr. And the most righteous (al-atqa) is more noble and better. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me, Abu Bakr and Umar.' He commanded Ali to follow them. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'They are the masters of the elderly of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of my nation is Abu Bakr, then Umar.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The sun has not risen nor set, after the prophets and messengers, on anyone better than Abu Bakr.' And there are many [similar statements]. And he said (peace be upon him): 'If there were to be a prophet after me, it would be Umar.' And he said: 'Should I not feel shy of one from whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' And this statement has been established from Ali, Ibn Umar, and Ibn al-Hanafiyya. And it is indicated by what has been widely transmitted of their deeds, reports, and efforts in Islam: bringing hearts together, successive conquests, suppressing the people of apostasy, breaking the Persians and Byzantines, conquering the East, subduing the foreign state, ordering affairs, dispensing justice, strengthening the weak, opening lands, raising the word of Allah, gathering people on a single Quran, equipping armies, spending wealth to support the religion, and so forth.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni proofs for the superiority (afdaliyya) of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman over Ali, though the author notes some hesitation between Uthman and Ali. The author first states the Sunni position: the order of caliphs is the order of superiority. He then gives two types of proof: summary and detailed. The summary proof is that the consensus of most scholars on this order indicates that they had evidence. The detailed proof begins with a Quranic verse (92:17-18) which is said to have been revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'al-atqa' implies the most righteous and best. Then several hadiths are cited: the Prophet's command to 'follow the two after me' (Abu Bakr and Umar), which includes Ali being commanded to follow them; the hadith that they are masters of the elderly of Paradise; the hadith that the best of the nation is Abu Bakr then Umar; a hadith that no one is better than Abu Bakr after the prophets; a hadith that if there were a prophet after him it would be Umar; and a hadith about Uthman that even angels feel shy of him. The author then mentions that these virtues are confirmed by the widely transmitted accounts of their deeds, such as uniting hearts, conquests, suppressing apostasy, etc. The passage ends with 'and so forth' indicating a longer list of achievements.

Key Terms
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni position on superiority is based only on consensus without textual evidence.
Correction: The author states that the consensus indicates there must be a proof, and he will later provide textual evidence from Quran and Hadith.

Checkpoint
What is the Sunni position on the ranking of superiority?
A) Ali is the best
B) The order of superiority follows the order of the caliphate
C) All caliphs are equal
D) Uthman is the best

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position holds that the caliphs' superiority follows the chronological order of their caliphate, and this is supported by the consensus of scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself preferring one of the caliphs over another based on personal bias or cultural upbringing rather than the evidence that the consensus of scholars points to? How can you submit your heart to the collective wisdom of the ummah even when it hesitates on some details?
?
This lesson uses the agreement of most scholars as a proof. Is your faith in the caliphs and their order rooted in your own reasoning or in trust of the scholarly consensus? What does that teach you about humility before the inherited tradition?
?
The hesitation between Uthman and Ali shows that even the great scholars did not have complete certainty on every ranking. How does this ambiguity challenge your need for absolute certainty in all matters of faith? Can you hold firm to the established order while remaining open to the limits of human knowledge?

Quranic Evidence for Abu Bakr's Superiority
Sanusi cites Quran 92:17-18 as proof for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'But the most righteous will be removed from it' is used as evidence for the superiority of Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The Quran calls Abu Bakr 'the most righteous,' implying his superiority.

The author now turns to detailed textual proofs, beginning with a specific Quranic verse that was revealed about Abu Bakr. By identifying Abu Bakr as 'the most righteous' (al-atqa), the verse establishes his preeminence over others.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Read the verse: 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself.'
This is verse 92:17-18.
2
Understand its application to Abu Bakr
The author states that this verse was revealed specifically about Abu Bakr.
3
Connect 'most righteous' to superiority
The term 'al-atqa' (the most righteous) implies the highest degree of righteousness, and thus superiority.
This is a linguistic inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: The sixth section — on divine superiority (al-afdaliyya). According to us (the Sunnis), [the order is] according to the sequence of the caliphate, with hesitation between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). According to the Shi'a and the majority of the Mu'tazila, Ali is superior (al-afdal). Our proof, in summary, is that the agreement of most scholars on that indicates the existence of a proof for them. And in detail, His saying (Exalted): 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself' (92:17-18). This was revealed about Abu Bakr. And the most righteous (al-atqa) is more noble and better. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me, Abu Bakr and Umar.' He commanded Ali to follow them. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'They are the masters of the elderly of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of my nation is Abu Bakr, then Umar.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The sun has not risen nor set, after the prophets and messengers, on anyone better than Abu Bakr.' And there are many [similar statements]. And he said (peace be upon him): 'If there were to be a prophet after me, it would be Umar.' And he said: 'Should I not feel shy of one from whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' And this statement has been established from Ali, Ibn Umar, and Ibn al-Hanafiyya. And it is indicated by what has been widely transmitted of their deeds, reports, and efforts in Islam: bringing hearts together, successive conquests, suppressing the people of apostasy, breaking the Persians and Byzantines, conquering the East, subduing the foreign state, ordering affairs, dispensing justice, strengthening the weak, opening lands, raising the word of Allah, gathering people on a single Quran, equipping armies, spending wealth to support the religion, and so forth.

This passage presents the Sunni proofs for the superiority (afdaliyya) of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman over Ali, though the author notes some hesitation between Uthman and Ali. The author first states the Sunni position: the order of caliphs is the order of superiority. He then gives two types of proof: summary and detailed. The summary proof is that the consensus of most scholars on this order indicates that they had evidence. The detailed proof begins with a Quranic verse (92:17-18) which is said to have been revealed about Abu Bakr, and 'al-atqa' implies the most righteous and best. Then several hadiths are cited: the Prophet's command to 'follow the two after me' (Abu Bakr and Umar), which includes Ali being commanded to follow them; the hadith that they are masters of the elderly of Paradise; the hadith that the best of the nation is Abu Bakr then Umar; a hadith that no one is better than Abu Bakr after the prophets; a hadith that if there were a prophet after him it would be Umar; and a hadith about Uthman that even angels feel shy of him. The author then mentions that these virtues are confirmed by the widely transmitted accounts of their deeds, such as uniting hearts, conquests, suppressing apostasy, etc. The passage ends with 'and so forth' indicating a longer list of achievements.

Key Terms
الأتقى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse is general and does not refer to Abu Bakr.
Correction: The author asserts that the verse was revealed specifically about Abu Bakr, based on reports of its occasion of revelation.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the author use as evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority?
A) 'Indeed, the righteous will be in pleasure.'
B) 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself.'
C) 'And the foremost are the foremost.'
D) 'And whoever is saved from the stinginess of his soul.'

Key Takeaway
The Quranic description of Abu Bakr as 'the most righteous' directly supports his superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse that Sanusi cites describes 'the most righteous' as one who gives his wealth to purify himself. In what area of your life are you holding back from spending for Allah's sake? What would it take for you to truly be among those who purify themselves through charity?
?
Abu Bakr's example of giving his wealth is tied to being called 'al-atqa'. Do you believe your own giving reflects a desire for righteousness or social approval? What one change can you make in your next charitable act to ensure it is purely for Allah?
?
If you truly believed that being 'the most righteous' is attainable through sincere action, what is holding you back from striving for that rank? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to increase your taqwa.

Prophetic Hadiths on the Superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar
Sanusi cites multiple hadiths proving the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know several hadiths that the Prophet used to indicate the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Core Idea
The Prophet commanded following Abu Bakr and Umar and praised them as the best of the nation.

The author presents a series of Prophetic hadiths that explicitly command following Abu Bakr and Umar, praise them as masters of Paradise, and rank them as the best of the Muslim community after the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the first hadith
The Prophet said: 'Follow the two after me, Abu Bakr and Umar.' This command includes Ali.
2
Read the second hadith
The Prophet said: 'They are the masters of the elderly of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.'
3
Read the third and fourth hadiths
The best of my nation is Abu Bakr, then Umar. And the sun has not risen nor set on anyone better than Abu Bakr after the prophets.
These explicitly rank them.
4
Note the abundance of similar hadiths
The author says 'there are many [similar statements]' indicating this is not an exhaustive list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث السادس - الله الأفضلية) عندنا بترتيب الخلافة مع تردد فيما بين عثمان وعلي (رضي عنهما). وعند الشيعة وجمهور المعتزلة الأفضل علي. لنا إجمالاً أن اتفاق أكثر العلماء على ذلك يقضي بوجود دليل لهم. وتفصيلا قوله تعالى وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتي ماله يتزكى (١) . نزلت في أبي بكر . والأتقى أكرم وأفضل. وقوله (عليه السلام): اقتدوا باللذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر (۲) فقد أمر على بالاقتداء بهما . وقوله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) هما سیدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين . وقوله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم) خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر . وقوله (عليه السلام ما طلعت الشمس ولا غربت بعد النبيين والمرسلين على أحد أفضل من أبي بكر. وفيها كثرة . وقال عليه السلام) : لو كان من بعدي نبي لكان عمر. وقال: «الا استحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء» (۱) وقد ثبت القول بهذا عن علي وابن عمر وابن الحنفية ودل عليه ما تواتر من آثارهم وأخبارهم ومساعيهم في الإسلام، ومن تألف القلوب وتتابع الفتوح، وقهر أهل الردة وكسر فارس والروم ومن فتح الشرق، وقمع دولة العجم وترتيب الأمور، وإفاضته العدل، وتقوية الضعفاء، ومن فتح البلاد وإعلاء كلمة الله وجمع الناس على مصحف واحد، وتجهيز الجيوش، وإنفاق الأموال في نصرة الدين، ونحو ذلك)
He said: The sixth section — on divine superiority (al-afdaliyya). According to us (the Sunnis), [the order is] according to the sequence of the caliphate, with hesitation between Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). According to the Shi'a and the majority of the Mu'tazila, Ali is superior (al-afdal). Our proof, in summary, is that the agreement of most scholars on that indicates the existence of a proof for them. And in detail, His saying (Exalted): 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself' (92:17-18). This was revealed about Abu Bakr. And the most righteous (al-atqa) is more noble and better. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me, Abu Bakr and Umar.' He commanded Ali to follow them. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'They are the masters of the elderly of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of my nation is Abu Bakr, then Umar.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The sun has not risen nor set, after the prophets and messengers, on anyone better than Abu Bakr.' And there are many [similar statements]. And he said (peace be upon him): 'If there were to be a prophet after me, it would be Umar.' And he said: 'Should I not feel shy of one from whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' And this statement has been established from Ali, Ibn Umar, and Ibn al-Hanafiyya. And it is indicated by what has been widely transmitted of their deeds, reports, and efforts in Islam: bringing hearts together, successive conquests, suppressing the people of apostasy, breaking the Persians and Byzantines, conquering the East, subduing the foreign state, ordering affairs, dispensing justice, strengthening the weak, opening lands, raising the word of Allah, gathering people on a single Quran, equipping armies, spending wealth to support the religion, and so forth.

Evidence for Umar and Uthman's Superiority and Transmitted Deeds
Sanusi cites hadiths for Umar and Uthman and appeals to transmitted deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadiths supporting the superiority of Umar and Uthman, and the cumulative evidence from their deeds.

Core Idea
Umar's high status is shown by the 'if there were a prophet' hadith, and Uthman's by the angels' shyness.

The author continues with specific hadiths about Umar and Uthman, and then emphasizes that the superior status of the caliphs is confirmed by the vast transmitted reports of their actions and achievements in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith about Umar
The Prophet said: 'If there were to be a prophet after me, it would be Umar.' This shows his near-prophetic rank.
2
Read the hadith about Uthman
The Prophet said: 'Should I not feel shy of one from whom the angels of heaven feel shy?' referring to Uthman.
3
Note the confirmation by prominent figures
These statements are confirmed by Ali, Ibn Umar, and Ibn al-Hanafiyya.
4
Consider the evidence from transmitted deeds
The widely transmitted reports of their achievements—conquests, justice, etc.—serve as additional proof of their superiority.
This is a cumulative argument.

The Sunni Order of the Caliphs and Its Certainty
The author explains the Sunni view on the order of the caliphs and cites Imam al-Haramayn's cautious stance on decisiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the order of the caliphs and why it is based on predominant probability (ghalib al-zann) rather than decisive certainty.

Core Idea
The Sunni order of the caliphs rests on strong probability, not absolute certainty.

This passage addresses a sensitive issue in Islamic theology: the ranking of the first four caliphs. The author presents the Sunni consensus, notes divergent opinions, and importantly cites Imam al-Haramayn who clarifies that the matter is not decisively proven but rests on predominant probability. Understanding this distinction helps avoid both dogmatic extremes and dismissive attitudes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the rule, the Sunni order, the internal disagreement, the quote from Imam al-Haramayn, the opposing view, and the Sunni proof.
2
Identify the Sunni order and internal disagreement
Note the Sunni order: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. Observe that some scholars preferred Ali over Uthman, while others suspended judgment. This shows the issue is not absolutely settled among Sunnis.
3
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's key point
Imam al-Haramayn states that the impossibility of the imamate of the inferior is not a decisive matter (laysat bi-qat'iyya). He adds that there is no decisive rational proof and that hadiths on virtues conflict. The predominant opinion is only probable.
This is the most important nuance in the passage.
4
Compare with the opposing view
The Shi'a and Mu'tazila hold that Ali is the best after the Prophet. Contrast this with the Sunni view and note how the author responds using consensus.
5
Reflect on the Sunni proof from consensus
The author argues that the consensus of the great scholars on the Sunni order is strong evidence, because they would not agree without proofs. This is a methodological point: consensus (ijma') is a valid source of knowledge in Sunni theology.
The detailed proofs are only mentioned in summary: Quran, Sunna, traditions, indications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما ذهب معظم أهل السنة، وكثير من الفرق على أنه يتعين للإمامة أفضل أهل العصر إلا إذا كان في نصبه مرج وهيجان فتن احتاجوا إلى بحث الأفضلية، فقال أهل السنة : الأفضل أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ، ثم علي . وقد مال البعض منهم إلى تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على عثمان، والبعض إلى التوقف فيما بينهما . قال إمام الحرمين : مسألة امتناع إمامة المفضول ليست بقطعية، ثم لا قاطع شاهد من العقل على تفضيل بعض الأئمة على البعض. والأخبار الواردة على فضائلهم متعارضة، لكن الغالب على الظن أن أبا بكر أفضل، ثم عمر . ثم يتعارض الظنون في عثمان وعلي رضي الله عنهما ) . وذهب الشيعة وجمهور المعتزلة إلى أن الأفضل بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم علي (رضي الله عنه). لنا إجمالاً أن جمهور عظماء الملة وعلماء الأمة أطبقوا على ذلك، وحسن الظن بهم يقضي بأنهم لو لم يعرفوه بدلائل وأمارات لما أطبقوا عليه . وتفصيلاً الكتاب ، والسنة ، والأثر، والأمارات .
Since most of the Ahl al-Sunna and many sects held that the best of the age is required for the imamate unless there is in his appointment commotion and upheaval of tribulation, they needed to discuss superiority (al-afdaliyya). Thus the Ahl al-Sunna said: The best is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. And some of them inclined to preferring Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) over Uthman, and some to suspending judgment between them. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'The issue of the impossibility of the imamate of the inferior is not decisive; moreover, there is no decisive rational proof for preferring some imams over others. The reports transmitted about their virtues are conflicting, but the predominant opinion (ghalib al-zann) is that Abu Bakr is best, then Umar. Then the opinions conflict regarding Uthman and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them).' The Shi'a and the majority of the Mu'tazila held that the best after the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Our proof in summary is that the majority of the great personages of the religious community and the scholars of the nation agreed upon that (the Sunni order), and good opinion of them necessitates that had they not known it through proofs and indications, they would not have agreed upon it. And in detail: the Book, the Sunna, the traditions (athar), and the indications.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the requirement for the imamate: the best person of the time should be appointed unless it would cause fitna. The author states the Sunni order of superiority: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, noting some divergence on Uthman vs. Ali. Then he quotes Imam al-Haramayn who says the matter is not decisive (qat'iyya); there is no clear rational proof, and hadiths on their virtues are conflicting. However, the predominant opinion (ghalib al-zann) is that Abu Bakr, then Umar, are best, while for Uthman and Ali, opinions conflict. Then the author contrasts the Shi'a/Mu'tazila view that Ali is best. Finally, he gives the Sunni response: the consensus of the great scholars of the community on the Sunni order is strong evidence, as their agreement must be based on proofs. The detailed proofs are then mentioned in brief: the Book, Sunna, traditions, and indications.

Key Terms
إمامة المفضول
قطعية
غالب الظن
جمهور عظماء الملة
إطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of the caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) is a matter of absolute certainty (qat'i) and cannot be questioned.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn explicitly states that the matter is not decisive (laysat bi-qat'iyya) and that there is no decisive rational proof; it is based on predominant probability (ghalib al-zann).

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Haramayn say about the issue of the imamate of the inferior (imamat al-mafdul)?
A) It is decisively prohibited.
B) It is not a decisive matter (qat'iyya).
C) It is allowed only if the superior is absent.
D) It is a matter of consensus among the scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni order of the caliphs is upheld by scholarly consensus and predominant probability, not by decisive textual or rational proof, as clarified by Imam al-Haramayn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the Sunni order of the caliphs, are you content to base your conviction on predominant probability (ghalib al-zann) rather than absolute certainty? What does this teach you about the nature of faith in historical matters?
?
Imam al-Haramayn admits that the reports are conflicting and there is no decisive rational proof. Does this disturb your trust in the Sunni position? How do you reconcile scholarly humility with your own certainty?
?
If the scholars themselves disagreed on the order of Uthman and Ali, how should that affect your attitude towards those who hold differing views within the Sunni fold? What is the heart of your allegiance—to the person or to the principle of unity?

Quranic Evidence for Abu Bakr's Superiority
The author cites Quranic evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority using verse 92:17-19.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 92:17-19 is used as evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority over Ali in classical Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The most righteous is the most honorable, and only Abu Bakr fits the verse's description.

In this passage, the author presents Quranic evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority. He cites a verse that describes someone who has no favor from anyone to be rewarded and explains why this applies to Abu Bakr and not to Ali. The reasoning hinges on the interpretation of key terms and the consensus of scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the Quranic verse and how it is interpreted.
Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the scholarly consensus
Observe that the author describes the majority opinion on the verse's revelation context.
This consensus is foundational to the argument.
3
Trace the reasoning linking righteousness to honor
The author connects 'al-atqa' to 'al-akram' using another verse. Understand why this equivalence is important.
It establishes that being 'best' (afdal) is based on righteousness/honor.
4
Understand why Ali is excluded
The author argues that Ali had a favor from the Prophet, so the verse's condition of 'no favor to be repaid' excludes him.
This eliminates Ali as a candidate for the verse's description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما الكتاب فقوله تعالى: ﴿ وسيجنبها الأتقى الذي يؤتى ماله يتزكى وما لأحد عنده من نعمة تجزى فالجمهور على أنها نزلت في أبي بكر رضي الله تعالى عنه والأتقى أكرم لقوله تعالى : إن أكرمكم عند الله أتقاكم ولا يعني بالأفضل إلا الأكرم ، وليس المراد به علياً، لأن للنبي عنده نعمة تجزى، وهي نعمة التربية .
As for the Book (Quran), His statement Exalted is He: 'And the most righteous will be removed from it, he who gives his wealth to purify himself, and who has no favor from anyone to be rewarded.' The majority (of scholars) hold that it was revealed about Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him. And 'al-atqa' (the most righteous) is the most honorable, as per His statement: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous.' And nothing is meant by 'the best' except 'the most honorable.' And it is not intended to be 'Ali, because the Prophet had a favor upon him that is to be rewarded, which is the favor of upbringing.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage begins the author's proof for Abu Bakr's superiority. He starts with the Quranic evidence. He cites the verse: 'Wesayujannabuha al-atqa...' (92:17-19). The majority of scholars hold that this verse was revealed about Abu Bakr. The author then explains that 'al-atqa' means 'the most honorable' (akram) based on another verse (49:13). He argues that 'the best' (al-afdal) here means 'the most honorable' (al-akram). He then refutes the claim that it refers to Ali, stating that Ali had a favor from the Prophet (the favor of upbringing and education), and the verse describes someone who has no favor from anyone to be rewarded. Therefore, it must refer to Abu Bakr.

Key Terms
الأتقى
الأكرم
الأفضل
أبو بكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'al-atqa' in the verse refers to Ali.
Correction: The author argues that Ali had a favor from the Prophet (upbringing), so he does not meet the condition of having no favor to be repaid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the verse 'and who has no favor from anyone to be rewarded' does not refer to Ali?
A) Because Ali was not righteous
B) Because Ali had a favor from the Prophet (upbringing)
C) Because the verse was revealed about Umar
D) Because Ali was not a companion

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 92:17-19, by scholarly consensus, refers to Abu Bakr and demonstrates his superiority as the most righteous and honorable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you give charity, do you secretly hope for recognition or repayment? The verse describes one who gives with no expectation of any favor returned. Examine your own giving: is it pure, like that of Abu Bakr?
?
The Prophet had a favor on Ali through his upbringing. Do you truly acknowledge the favors of those who taught you, or do you sometimes feel independent? How can you show gratitude today?
?
This passage defines true honor as righteousness. Are you more concerned with your reputation among people or your standing with Allah? What concrete step can you take today to increase your taqwa?

The Argument from the Command to Follow
The first hadith and its logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inference from the hadith 'Follow the two after me' that proves Abu Bakr and Umar's superiority over Ali.

Core Idea
The command to follow implies inferiority of the follower.

This lesson examines the first hadith presented by the author as Sunnah evidence. The Prophet's command to follow Abu Bakr and Umar includes Ali, from which the author derives a powerful argument for their superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic hadith and its literal translation once to grasp the surface meaning.
The hadith is short but packed with implication.
2
Understand the logical reasoning
The author argues that Ali is included in the command to follow, and one does not command a superior or equal to follow.
This is a rational inference from the text.
3
Note the sectarian context
The author adds 'especially according to the Shi'a' to highlight the refutation of their claim of Ali's superiority.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument counters opposing views and establishes the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السنة فقوله ( عليه السلام) اقتدوا باللذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر دخل في الخطاب علي رضي الله عنه فيكون مأموراً بالاقتداء ولا يؤمر الأفضل ولا المساوي بالاقتداء، سيما عند الشيعة. وقوله لأبي بكر وعمر هما سيدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين وقوله (عليه السلام) : خير أمتي أبو بكر ثم عمر. وقوله (عليه السلام): ما ينبغي لقوم فيهم أبو بكر أن يتقدم عليه عنده . وقوله ( ) : لو كنت متخذاً خليلاً دون ربي لا تخذت أبا بكر خليلاً، ولكن هو شريك في ديني وصاحبي الذي أوجبت له صحبتي في الغار، وخليفتي في أمتي ) وقوله : وأين مثل أبي بكر ، كذبني الناس وصدقني، وآمن بي وزوجني ابنته، وجهز لي بماله، واساني بنفسه، وجاهد معي ساعة الخوف. وقوله ( ) لأبي الدرداء حين كان يمشي أمام أبي بكر: أتمشي أمام من هو خير منك؟ والله ما طلعت شمس ولا غربت بعد النبيين والمرسلين على أحد أفضل من أبي بكر.
As for the Sunnah, his statement (peace be upon him): 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is included in the address, so he is commanded to follow, and one does not command the superior or the equal to follow, especially according to the Shi'a. And his statement about Abu Bakr and Umar: 'They are the masters of the mature people of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'The best of my ummah is Abu Bakr, then Umar.' And his statement (peace be upon him): 'It is not befitting for a people among whom is Abu Bakr that anyone else should lead them in prayer.' And his statement ( ): 'If I were to take a close friend (khalil) besides my Lord, I would take Abu Bakr as a close friend, but he is my partner in my religion, my companion for whom I obligated his companionship in the cave, and my caliph over my ummah.' And his statement: 'Where is anyone like Abu Bakr? He denied me when people denied me, he believed me, he married his daughter to me, he equipped me with his wealth, he consoled me with himself, and he struggled with me in the hour of fear.' And his statement ( ) to Abu al-Darda' when he was walking in front of Abu Bakr: 'Do you walk in front of one who is better than you? By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set after the prophets and messengers upon anyone better than Abu Bakr.'
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunnah evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority. The author lists several hadiths. First, the Prophet said, 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' The author argues that Ali is included in this command, and one does not command a superior or an equal to follow; therefore, Abu Bakr and Umar are superior to Ali. This is especially significant against the Shi'a. Second, the Prophet called Abu Bakr and Umar the masters of the mature people of Paradise, except for prophets. Third, the Prophet declared Abu Bakr the best of his ummah, then Umar. Fourth, the Prophet said that when Abu Bakr is present, no one else should lead the prayer. Fifth, the Prophet stated that if he were to take a close friend, he would take Abu Bakr, but instead Abu Bakr is his partner in religion, his companion in the cave, and his caliph. Sixth, the Prophet listed Abu Bakr's virtues: he believed him when others denied, gave his daughter in marriage, provided wealth, etc. Seventh, the Prophet told Abu al-Darda' that walking in front of Abu Bakr is inappropriate because Abu Bakr is better than him, and that no one after the prophets is better than Abu Bakr. These hadiths collectively establish Abu Bakr's superiority.

Key Terms
اقتدوا
خليفتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Shi'a believe Ali was superior to Abu Bakr and Umar from the beginning.
Correction: The hadith and its logical implication show Ali was commanded to follow, which would not happen if he were superior.

Checkpoint
According to the author's argument, what does the hadith 'Follow the two after me' imply about Ali?
A) Ali is not commanded to follow
B) Ali is commanded to follow, proving Abu Bakr and Umar are superior
C) The hadith is not authentic
D) It applies only to later generations

Key Takeaway
The command to follow Abu Bakr and Umar includes Ali and proves their superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet's command to follow Abu Bakr and Umar includes Ali, who is so highly regarded, then what excuse do you have for not submitting to the same command? Is there any person you consider too great to follow after the Prophet? Examine your heart—is there pride that resists the clear directive of the Prophet?
?
The Shi'a might claim Ali's command to follow was a test, but the source insists that commanding an equal or superior to follow is improper. When you are commanded to follow someone in religious matters, do you accept it based on textual evidence even if it challenges your personal estimation of that person's worth? Are you willing to follow those whom the Prophet designated, or do you let your own opinions override divine command?
?
This argument uses logic to establish superiority. In your life, when you encounter a command from Allah or His Prophet that contradicts your own sense of fairness or hierarchy, do you submit to it, or do you rationalize it away? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that the Prophet's commands are not to be questioned but obeyed, even when they go against your own preferences?

Explicit Statements of Superiority
Three hadiths explicitly stating superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn three hadiths that explicitly state the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar over the rest of the ummah.

Core Idea
The Prophet explicitly called Abu Bakr and Umar the best and masters.

This lesson covers three hadiths that leave no room for doubt: Abu Bakr and Umar are the masters of Paradise's mature people, the best of the ummah, and the rightful leaders of prayer when present.

Guided Path
1
Read the three hadiths and translations
Read each hadith carefully and understand its literal meaning.
These are direct statements from the Prophet.
2
Notice the superlatives used
The hadiths use words like 'masters', 'best', and 'not befitting' to indicate highest rank.
3
Understand the significance of prayer leadership
The hadith about prayer leadership shows that Abu Bakr takes precedence in religious authority.
4
Consider the order of precedence
The second hadith explicitly ranks Abu Bakr first, then Umar, implying a hierarchy.

Virtues and Close Companionship
Multiple hadiths highlighting Abu Bakr's virtues and the Prophet's high praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadiths that highlight Abu Bakr's unique virtues and close relationship with the Prophet.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's unparalleled sacrifices and companionship earn him the highest rank.

The final set of hadiths presents Abu Bakr's personal virtues: his belief when others denied, his marriage to the Prophet's daughter, his financial support, his companionship in the cave, and the Prophet's statement that none after prophets is better than him.

1
Read the hadiths and translations
Read each hadith and its translation to understand the specific virtues mentioned.
These hadiths enumerate Abu Bakr's unique merits.
2
Understand the 'khalil' hadith
The Prophet's statement about not taking a close friend but still honoring Abu Bakr highlights his special status.
3
List Abu Bakr's virtues from the second hadith
Count the virtues: early belief, marriage tie, wealth, consolation, struggle.
4
Reflect on the Prophet's testimony to Abu al-Darda'
The Prophet declares Abu Bakr the best after prophets, confirming his superiority.
