Resolving the Apparent Problem in the Hadith on Tasbih
Resolves an apparent contradiction in the hadith about saying tasbih 100 times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent negation of superiority in certain prophetic statements is actually intended to affirm the superiority of the mentioned person, and how this principle resolves the difficulty in the hadith on tasbih.

Core Idea
When the Prophet appears to deny someone's superiority, he is really affirming another's.

This passage uses two examples—the praise of Abu Bakr and the hadith about tasbih—to teach a key principle: that negation of superiority can be a rhetorical tool for affirmation. Understanding this resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's flow come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the two examples: Abu Bakr and the tasbih hadith.
2
Identify the two examples
Find where the Prophet praises Abu Bakr and where he condemns Abu Darda. Then find the hadith about saying 'Subhan Allah wa bihamdihi' 100 times.
3
Understand the principle
The author states that statements like 'where is the likes of Abu Bakr' are actually for affirming his superiority, not negating others. Grasp the underlying logic about differentiation versus equality.
The key sentence: 'السر في ذلك أن الغالب من حال كل اثنين هو التفاضل دون التساوي'.
4
Apply to the tasbih hadith
See how the exception in the tasbih hadith is interpreted: it appears to negate the doer's superiority but actually affirms it, because the exception is from negation not affirmation.
The author says: 'الاستثناء بظاهره من النفي، وبالتحقيق من الإثبات'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وأين مثل أبي بكر ، كذبني الناس وصدقني، وآمن بي وزوجني ابنته، وجهز لي بماله، واساني بنفسه، وجاهد معي ساعة الخوف. وقوله ( ) لأبي الدرداء حين كان يمشي أمام أبي بكر: أتمشي أمام من هو خير منك؟ والله ما طلعت شمس ولا غربت بعد النبيين والمرسلين على أحد أفضل من أبي بكر. ومثل هذا الكلام وإن كان ظاهره نفي أفضلية الغير، لكن إنما يساق لإثبات أفضلية المذكور. ولهذا أفاد أن أبا بكر أفضل من أبي الدرداء. والسر في ذلك أن الغالب من حال كل اثنين هو التفاضل دون التساوي، فإذا نفى أفضلية أحدهما لآخر، ثبت أفضلية الآخر. وبمثل هذا ينحل الإشكال المشهور على قوله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : «من قال حين يصبح وحين يمسي: سبحان الله وبحمده مائة مرة لم يأت أحد يوم القيامة بأفضل مما جاء به إلا أحد قال مثل ما قال أو زاد عليه» لأنه في معنى أن من قال ذلك فقد أتى بأفضل مما جاء به كل أحد إلا احداً قال مثل ذلك أو زاد عليه . فالاستثناء بظاهره من النفي، وبالتحقيق من الإثبات.
And His saying: 'And where is the likes of Abu Bakr? The people denied me and he believed me, and he believed in me and married me to his daughter, and equipped me with his wealth, and consoled me with himself, and struggled with me at the hour of fear.' And His saying ( ) to Abu Darda when he was walking in front of Abu Bakr: 'Do you walk in front of one who is better than you? By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set after the prophets and messengers on anyone better than Abu Bakr.' And such speech, although its apparent meaning is the negation of the superiority of others, but it is only brought to affirm the superiority of the mentioned [person]. And for this reason it indicates that Abu Bakr is better than Abu Darda. And the secret in that is that the prevailing condition of any two [persons] is differentiation rather than equality. So if the superiority of one over another is negated, the superiority of the other is established. And by this is resolved the well-known problem regarding his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever says when he becomes morning and when he becomes evening: Glory be to Allah and His praise one hundred times, no one will come on the Day of Resurrection with anything better than what he came with, except one who said the like of what he said or increased upon it.' Because it is in the meaning that whoever says that has brought something better than what everyone brought, except one who said the like of that or increased upon it. So the exception in its apparent meaning is from the negation, but in reality it is from the affirmation.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage analyzes two prophetic sayings to demonstrate a principle of interpretation: when a statement apparently negates the superiority of others, it is often intended to affirm the superiority of the one mentioned. The author begins with the Prophet's praise of Abu Bakr: 'And where is the likes of Abu Bakr?' followed by a list of Abu Bakr's unique virtues. Then he cites the incident with Abu Darda, where the Prophet rebukes him for walking ahead of Abu Bakr, explicitly stating that no one after the prophets is better than Abu Bakr. The author notes that although such statements appear to negate the superiority of others (by saying 'where is the likes of...' or 'you are not better than him'), their purpose is actually to affirm the superiority of the one being spoken about. The secret underlying this is that when two people are compared, the default is differentiation (one is better) not equality. So if you negate the superiority of one over the other, you affirm the superiority of the other. This principle then resolves a well-known difficulty in another hadith: the one about saying 'Subhan Allah wa bihamdihi' 100 times morning and evening. In that hadith, the Prophet says that no one will come with something better except the one who said the same or more. The apparent meaning is that the exception proves that someone else can be equal or better. But the author explains that the exception is actually from the negation, meaning 'only the one who says the same or more will bring something better', which effectively affirms that the person who said the tasbih has brought the best, unless another does the same. The exception is from negation in wording but from affirmation in reality.

Key Terms
تفاضل
استثناء
نفي
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith on tasbih implies that someone else can be equal or better, contradicting its apparent meaning.
Correction: The exception is actually from the negation, affirming that the doer brings the best unless another does the same.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is the exception in the hadith 'من قال سبحان الله وبحمده...' to be understood?
A) The exception is from affirmation, so only the one who says it is best.
B) The exception is from negation, so others can be equal or better.
C) The exception is from both negation and affirmation.
D) The exception is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
Understanding the rhetorical principle of negation for affirmation resolves apparent contradictions in prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith about tasbih, did you feel a sense of complacency, thinking others can easily match your deed? How does the correct understanding challenge you to take your worship more seriously, knowing that this dhikr sets a high standard?
?
The Prophet rebuked Abu Darda for walking ahead of Abu Bakr. Is there someone in your life—a parent, a teacher, a spouse—whom you fail to honor as they deserve? What concrete step will you take today to correct that?
?
The author says the default between two people is differentiation, not equality. In your heart, do you secretly assume you are superior to others? How can this lesson help you humble yourself and recognize the unique virtues in those around you?

Hadiths on the Love and Excellence of Abu Bakr and Umar
Three hadiths demonstrating the Prophet's love for Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three prophetic hadiths establish the high rank of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Core Idea
The Prophet's love and praise for Abu Bakr and Umar reveal their supreme station in Islam.

This lesson presents three hadiths that the author cites to demonstrate the love and excellence of Abu Bakr and Umar. These narrations come from the Prophet himself, showing his personal affection and their unique roles. Through these texts, we see the rank of the two greatest companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the three hadiths
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the different contexts: a conversation with Amr ibn al-As, a direct statement about Umar, and a metaphorical description.
Each hadith adds a distinct dimension to their excellence.
2
Analyze the hierarchy of love
In the first hadith, the Prophet lists Aisha, then her father Abu Bakr, then Umar. Observe that this establishes a clear order of love.
This shows Abu Bakr's precedence over Umar.
3
Understand the conditional praise of Umar
The second hadith says if there were a prophet after Muhammad, it would be Umar. This is hyperbolic praise, not literal, as prophethood is sealed.
This highlights Umar's extraordinary virtue.
4
Reflect on the metaphor of hearing and sight
The Prophet calls Abu Bakr and Umar 'hearing and sight,' implying they are essential, complementary senses for the community.
This metaphor emphasizes their indispensable roles.
5
Consider the implications
These hadiths are used in Sunni theology to affirm the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar over other companions.
They are evidence for the ranking of the caliphs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن عمرو بن العاص، قلت لرسول الله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أي الناس أحب إليك؟ قال : عائشة . قلت : من الرجال؟ قال أبوها ، قلت : ثم من؟ قال عمر. وقال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) لو كان بعدي نبي لكان عمر. وعن عبد الله بن حنطب أن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) رأى أبا بكر وعمر فقال : هذان السمع والبصر.
And from Amr ibn al-As: I said to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): Which people are most beloved to you? He said: Aisha. I said: Among men? He said: Her father. I said: Then who? He said: Umar. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: If there were to be a prophet after me, it would be Umar. And from Abdullah ibn Hanzab that the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw Abu Bakr and Umar and said: These two are hearing and sight.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents three hadiths that collectively establish the high rank of Abu Bakr and Umar. The first hadith, from Amr ibn al-As, shows the Prophet's personal love: when asked about the most beloved people to him, he mentions Aisha first (among women) and then her father Abu Bakr (among men), followed by Umar. This establishes a clear hierarchy of love. The second hadith is a direct statement of Umar's unique station: if prophecy were to continue after the Prophet, Umar would be a prophet. This is a powerful indication of his excellence, even though prophecy is sealed. The third hadith, from Abdullah ibn Hanzab, is a metaphorical description: the Prophet compares Abu Bakr and Umar to 'hearing and sight', implying they are essential, complementary senses of the community, and by extension, that they are the most important companions after him.

Key Terms
أحب
نبي
السمع والبصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith about Umar being a potential prophet implies that prophethood could continue after Muhammad.
Correction: The statement is a conditional hyperbole showing Umar's great virtue; the finality of prophethood is a core Islamic belief (Quran 33:40), so it cannot be literal.

Checkpoint
What is implied by the Prophet's statement about Umar: 'لو كان بعدي نبي لكان عمر'?
A) Umar actually became a prophet after him.
B) Umar's rank is so high that if anyone could be a prophet after Muhammad, it would be Umar.
C) The Prophet was unsure about the finality of prophethood.
D) Umar was a prophet in the past.

Key Takeaway
These three hadiths collectively affirm the unparalleled love and excellence of Abu Bakr and Umar, the first two caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet's love for Abu Bakr and Umar was based on their faith and character. Who are the people in your life that you love most? Is your love for them rooted in their devotion to Allah and His Messenger? Prepare to re-evaluate your relationships.
?
If you were asked ‘Who is most beloved to you among the believers?’, would your answer reflect the Prophet's priorities? What does your list of beloved people say about your own heart? Commit to adjusting your affections toward those who are more pious.
?
The Prophet likened Abu Bakr and Umar to hearing and sight—indispensable senses. Do you consider the companions as indispensable guides for your faith? Or do you ignore their example? Make a concrete plan to learn from their lives this week.

Athar Reports on the Ranking of the Three Caliphs
Reports from Ibn Umar, Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, and Ali on the order of the best companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three authoritative reports (athar) from Ibn Umar, Ali ibn Abi Talib, and his son Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya affirm the ranking of the first three caliphs as the best of the ummah after the Prophet.

Core Idea
Reports from the Companions themselves confirm the order of the best: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman.

The discussion of who is best among the Companions is not merely a historical curiosity; it has profound theological implications for understanding the legitimacy of the caliphate and the unity of the ummah. The three reports presented here come from key figures: Ibn Umar, who testifies that this ranking was known during the Prophet's lifetime; Ali, whose humility is evident; and his son Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. These reports serve as primary evidence for the Sunni position on the excellence of the caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the reports carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of all three reports. Notice that they all affirm the same order, but with different details and contexts.
2
Identify the key figures and their testimonies
For each report, note who is speaking and what they say. Pay attention to the subtle differences: Ibn Umar says they said it while the Prophet was alive; Ali says 'then Allah knows best'; Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya's report shows Ali's humility.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: Why are these reports important for proving the Sunni view on the caliphs? How do they counter alternative views? The commentary will clarify.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأثر، فعن ابن عمر، كنا نقول ورسول الله حي : أفضل أمة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) بعده أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وعن محمد ابن الحنفية قلت لأبي أي الناس خير بعد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : أبو بكر قلت : ثم من؟ قال : عمر. وخشيت أن أقول ثم من، فيقول عثمان. فقلت : ثم أنت قال ما أنا إلا رجل من المسلمين. وعن علي (رضي الله عنه : خير الناس بعد النبيين أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم الله أعلم . وعنه (رضي الله عنه) لما قيل له : ما توصي؟ قال : ما أوصى رسول الله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم ) أوصي. ولكن إن أراد الله بالناس خيراً جمعهم على خيرهم كما جمعهم بعد نبيهم على خيرهم.
As for the report (athar): from Ibn Umar: We used to say while the Messenger of Allah was alive: The best of the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) after him is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. And from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya: I said to my father: Who is the best of people after the Prophet (peace be upon him)? He said: Abu Bakr. I said: Then who? He said: Umar. And I feared to say Then who, so he would say Uthman. So I said: Then you? He said: I am only a man from the Muslims. And from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): The best of people after the prophets is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Allah knows best. And from him (may Allah be pleased with him) when it was said to him: What do you advise? He said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not advise [me] so I advise. But if Allah wants good for the people, He will unite them upon their best as He united them after their Prophet upon their best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents three reports (athar) from authoritative Companions and Successors affirming the ranking of the best of the ummah after the Prophet. The first report is from Ibn Umar, who says that during the Prophet's lifetime, they used to say that the best of the ummah after him is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. This indicates that this ranking was established even while the Prophet was alive, presumably with his tacit approval. The second report is from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, the son of Ali. He asked his father (Ali) about the best of people after the Prophet, and Ali answered: Abu Bakr. When asked next, he said Umar. Muhammad then feared to ask further because he expected his father to say Uthman (as the third), and then he himself would be asked. He avoided putting his father in an awkward position. Ali's response shows his own belief in the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar. When pressed about himself, Ali humbly says 'I am only a man from the Muslims.' The third report is directly from Ali, stating that the best of people after the prophets is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Allah knows best. This shows his deference to divine knowledge regarding the exact order thereafter. Another version from Ali advises unity: when asked for advice, he declines to give a specific testament, but says that if Allah wants good for people, He will unite them upon their best, just as He united them after their Prophet upon their best (meaning Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman). This implies that the community's unity depends on accepting the established consensus on the caliphs' excellence.

Key Terms
أثر
أفضل
خير الناس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ali's phrase 'ثم الله أعلم' indicates he was uncertain about the ranking of the caliphs or disagreed with Abu Bakr and Umar being superior.
Correction: In fact, Ali's statement is a pious expression of leaving the exact ordering after Umar to Allah, affirming his acceptance of the first two as best. The context shows he deliberately named Abu Bakr and Umar and then deferred to divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
What was Ali ibn Abi Talib's response when asked about the best people after the Prophet?
A) He named his own sons Hasan and Husayn.
B) He said Abu Bakr, then Umar, then himself.
C) He said Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Allah knows best.
D) He refused to answer.

Key Takeaway
Three authoritative reports from Ibn Umar, Ali, and his son confirm that the best of the ummah after the Prophet are Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, establishing a clear consensus among the early community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali, when asked about himself, said 'I am only a man from the Muslims.' When was the last time you genuinely considered yourself no better than any other Muslim, regardless of your achievements? What would change in your daily interactions if you truly felt that way?
?
The reports show that the ranking of the best Companions was known even during the Prophet's lifetime. Do you have any attachment to a particular scholar or figure that might prevent you from accepting the consensus of the early community? Are you willing to submit to that consensus, even if it challenges your personal preferences?
?
Ibn Umar said 'We used to say...' indicating the ranking was common knowledge. What beliefs do you hold so strongly that you would dare to say them in the presence of the Prophet (peace be upon him)? Would you be ashamed if he heard your current opinions about the Companions?

Indications of the Virtues of the First Three Caliphs
Lists historical achievements as signs of the caliphs' divinely favored status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how historical achievements and prophetic honors serve as indications (amarat) of the superior virtue of the first three caliphs in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Visible signs of divine favor confirm the caliphs' virtues.

In this passage, the author presents a cumulative argument from historical events and personal qualities as indications of Allah's approval of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. These are not absolute proofs but strong signs that support the Sunni position on their superiority. Let's examine the specific achievements and honors listed for each caliph.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'amarat'
Note that 'amarat' means indications, not definitive proofs. The author uses this term to suggest that these are visible manifestations of divine favor, not demonstrative arguments.
The word 'tawatara' (successively reported) for Abu Bakr's era implies historical certainty.
2
Examine the indications for each caliph
Read the passage section by section for Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. Identify the specific achievements and honors listed for each.
Abu Bakr's indications focus on unification and conquest; Umar's on expansion and justice; Uthman's on consolidation and personal merit.
3
Reflect on the cumulative argument
Consider how these separate sets of signs together build a case for the caliphs' virtue. Ask yourself: How do these historical facts support the Sunni view against opposing claims?
The author does not prove superiority by a single event but by a collection of widely reported signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأمارات فما تواتر في أيام أبي بكر من اجتماع الكلمة، وتألف القلوب وتتابع الفتوح وقهر أهل الردة وتطهير جزيرة العرب عن الشرك، وإجلاء الروم عن الشام وأطرافها وطرد فارس عن حدود السواد وأطراف العراق مع قوتهم وشوكتهم ووفور أموالهم، وانتظام أحوالهم. وفي أيام عمر من فتح جانب المشرق إلى أقصى خراسان، وقطع دولة العجم وثل عرشهم الراسي البنيان، الثابث الأركان . ومن ترتيب الأمور، وسياسة الجمهور، وإفاضة العدل، وتقوية الضعفاء، ومن إعراضه من متاع الدنيا وطيباتها وملاذها وشهواتها. وفي أيام عثمان من فتح البلاد وإعلاء لواء الإسلام، وجمع الناس على مصحف واحد مع ما كان له من الورع والتقوى، وتجهيز جيوش المسلمين، والإنفاق في نصرة الدين والمهاجرة هجرتين، وكونه ختناً للنبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) على ابنتين ، والاستحياء من أدنى شين، وتشرفه بقوله ( عليه السلام عثمان أخي ورفيقي في الجنة ، وقوله : ألا أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء . وقوله ( ) : إنه رجل يدخل الجنة بغير حساب .
As for the indications (amarat): what has been successively reported from the days of Abu Bakr of unification of the word, bonding of hearts, successive conquests, subduing the people of apostasy, purifying the Arabian Peninsula from polytheism, expelling the Romans from Syria and its borders, driving the Persians from the borders of Sawad and the borders of Iraq despite their strength, might, abundance of wealth, and organization of their affairs. And from the days of Umar: conquering the eastern side as far as the furthest Khurasan, cutting off the state of the non-Arabs, toppling their established and firmly founded throne, and from the arrangement of affairs, governance of the populace, spreading justice, strengthening the weak, and his turning away from the goods of this world and its delights, pleasures, and desires. And from the days of Uthman: conquering lands, raising the banner of Islam, gathering the people upon a single Mushaf along with what he had of piety and God-fearing, equipping the Muslim armies, spending in support of the religion, migrating twice, being a son-in-law of the Prophet (peace be upon him) through two daughters, shyness from the least disgrace, being honored by his saying (peace be upon him): 'Uthman is my brother and companion in Paradise', and his saying: 'Should I not be shy of one whom the angels of heaven are shy of?' and his saying (peace be upon him): 'He is a man who will enter Paradise without reckoning.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents a cumulative argument from historical signs or indications (amarat) for the superior virtue and divine favor of the first three Caliphs. The author uses the term 'amarat' (indications) rather than definitive proofs, suggesting these are visible manifestations of Allah's approval. For Abu Bakr, the indications include: unifying the Muslims after the Prophet's death, bonding hearts (which alludes to the gathering of the Companions around him), successive conquests, suppression of the apostasy wars (ridda), purification of Arabia from outward polytheism, and expelling the Byzantine and Persian empires from the borders of the Islamic state—all accomplished despite the enemy's strength. For Umar, the indications include: vast conquests extending to Khurasan, the destruction of the Sassanid Persian empire (the 'state of the non-Arabs'), toppling their strong throne, and his administrative achievements like arranging affairs, governing the populace, spreading justice, and strengthening the weak. Personal asceticism is also noted: his turning away from worldly pleasures. For Uthman, the indications include: continued conquests, raising the banner of Islam, standardizing the Qur'anic text (the single Mushaf), his personal piety and fear of Allah, equipping armies (especially the expedition of Tabuk), spending for the religion, his double migration (to Abyssinia and Medina), being the Prophet's son-in-law through two daughters (Ruqayya and Umm Kulthum), his extreme modesty, and several prophetic honors: being called the Prophet's brother and companion in Paradise, the Prophet's shyness before him (as the angels are shy), and the promise that he will enter Paradise without reckoning. The author uses the word 'tawatara' (successively reported) for the events of Abu Bakr's era, implying that these are well-established historical facts. The cumulative effect of these achievements and honors is presented as a strong indication of the superiority and divine pleasure with these three caliphs, in support of the Sunni position against the Shia view that Ali was superior.

Key Terms
أمارات
تواتر
فتوح
ردة
مصحف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These indications are absolute proofs (burhan) of the caliphs' superiority.
Correction: The author explicitly calls them 'amarat' (indications), not definitive proofs, and relies on their cumulative weight rather than demonstrative argument.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of listing these 'amarat' (indications) for the three caliphs?
A) To prove their prophethood.
B) To provide historical facts without theological significance.
C) To support the Sunni claim of their superiority through visible signs of divine favor.
D) To argue that their reigns were perfect and without any problems.

Key Takeaway
Historical achievements and prophetic honors, transmitted as well-known facts, provide strong indications (amarat) of the divine favor and superior virtue of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the achievements of the first three caliphs, do you see them as mere historical facts or as signs of Allah's pleasure? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that these events were indications of divine favor?
?
The caliphs are presented with personal virtues like turning away from worldly pleasures (Umar) and extreme modesty (Uthman). Which of these virtues do you lack most, and what specific step will you take this week to cultivate it?
?
If you were to be judged by the indications of your own life—your actions, sacrifices, and the honors you seek—what would they say about your faith? Are you building a legacy that points to Allah's pleasure, or one that points to your own?

Reports on the Best of the Ummah After the Prophet
Reports from Companions on the best after the Prophet, including Ali's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary narrations from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that establish the hierarchy of virtue (afḍaliyya) among the Companions, particularly Abu Bakr and 'Umar.

Core Idea
'Ali himself affirmed that Abu Bakr and 'Umar are the best of this Ummah after the Prophet.

This passage presents key reports from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that establish the order of excellence among the Companions. These narrations serve as primary evidence for the Sunni position on the caliphate and the virtue of the first three caliphs. Let's examine what 'Ali said when asked directly about the best after the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the different reports contained within.
2
Identify the three reports
Find the initial summary statement, the conversation with Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, and the two direct statements from 'Ali.
The passage opens with a concise summary before detailing the reports.
3
Analyze 'Ali's responses
Observe that 'Ali names Abu Bakr and 'Umar explicitly, but hesitates to name 'Uthman and declines to name himself, showing modesty.
His modesty is evident when he says 'I am but a man among the Muslims.'
4
Understand the theological significance
These reports provide strong evidence that the Sunni belief in the superiority of Abu Bakr and 'Umar is rooted in the words of the Companions themselves, particularly 'Ali.
This counters sectarian claims that 'Ali was the best after the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان . وعن محمد ابن الحنفية قلت لأبي أي الناس خير بعد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : أبو بكر قلت : ثم من؟ قال : عمر. وخشيت أن أقول ثم من، فيقول عثمان. فقلت : ثم أنت قال ما أنا إلا رجل من المسلمين. وعن علي (رضي الله عنه : خير الناس بعد النبيين أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم الله أعلم . وعنه (رضي الله عنه) لما قيل له : ما توصي؟ قال : ما أوصى رسول الله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم ) أوصي. ولكن إن أراد الله بالناس خيراً جمعهم على خيرهم كما جمعهم بعد نبيهم على خيرهم .
Abu Bakr then 'Umar then 'Uthman. And from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya: I said to my father: Who is the best of people after the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? He said: Abu Bakr. I said: Then who? He said: 'Umar. And I feared to say then who, so he would say 'Uthman. So I said: Then you? He said: I am but a man among the Muslims. And from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): The best of people after the prophets are Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then Allah knows best. And from him (may Allah be pleased with him) when it was said to him: What do you advise? He said: The Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) did not advise me. But if Allah intends good for the people, He will unite them upon their best, just as He united them after their Prophet upon their best.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage reports narrations establishing the order of virtue (afḍaliyya) among the Companions after the Prophet. It first cites Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya asking his father 'Ali about the best people after the Prophet. 'Ali answers: Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, but refrains from mentioning 'Uthman due to modesty, only identifying himself as a common Muslim. Then a direct statement from 'Ali clarifies: the best after the prophets are Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, and then 'Ali leaves the next best to Allah's knowledge. Finally, 'Ali advises that if Allah wills good for the people, He will unite them upon their best, referencing how unity was achieved after the Prophet's death around the best of them (implying Abu Bakr). These reports serve as primary evidence for the Sunni position on the hierarchy of the caliphs.

Key Terms
أفضلية (afḍaliyya)
الصحابة (al-ṣaḥābah)
الخلافة (al-khilāfah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'Ali believed someone else was better than himself, or that he claimed superiority over Abu Bakr and 'Umar.
Correction: The reports show that 'Ali explicitly named Abu Bakr and 'Umar as the best after the Prophet, and he humbly considered himself just a man among Muslims. He did not claim superiority over them.

Checkpoint
According to the report from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, who did 'Ali name as the best after the Prophet?
A) 'Uthman and 'Ali
B) Abu Bakr and 'Umar
C) Abu Bakr only
D) 'Ali and Abu Bakr

Key Takeaway
'Ali's own words confirm that after the prophets, the best of people are Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, leaving the next to Allah's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When 'Ali was asked about the best after the Prophet, he immediately named Abu Bakr and 'Umar, not himself. Is your heart truly free from any sense of self-importance when you consider your own status among the believers? What would you say if you were asked who is better than you in faith?
?
If Allah united the Ummah upon their best after the Prophet, then today's divisions among Muslims reflect a departure from that divine pattern. Are you working to unite with others upon the truth, or are you contributing to the disunity by holding onto personal opinions?
?
'Ali said, 'I am but a man among the Muslims.' Do you live with this same humility, or do you secretly believe that your knowledge, practice, or lineage makes you superior to other believers? How can you actively combat that feeling today?

Indications of Abu Bakr's Virtue
Signs from Abu Bakr's caliphate as evidence of virtue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical indications from Abu Bakr's caliphate that demonstrate his virtue, as presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's reign unified Arabia and launched Islam's expansion.

Sanusi presents concrete historical events from Abu Bakr's caliphate as signs of Allah's pleasure. These include unifying the community, defeating apostasy, and beginning the conquests. Let's examine each indication as a window into his leadership and divine favor.

Guided Path
1
Read the indications for Abu Bakr
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the signs from Abu Bakr's era. Notice the sequence: unity, conquests, purification, expelling empires.
The phrase 'تألف القلوب' shows emotional unity.
2
Reflect on the historical context
Consider the challenge of apostasy (ahl al-riddah) after the Prophet's death. Abu Bakr's success was not merely political but a preservation of faith.
3
Connect to the broader argument
These signs are not random achievements; they are external proofs (qarā'in) of his virtue, alongside consensus and direct texts.
Consider how these events are depicted as divinely ordained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأمارات فما تواتر في أيام أبي بكر من اجتماع الكلمة، وتألف القلوب وتتابع الفتوح وقهر أهل الردة وتطهير جزيرة العرب عن الشرك، وإجلاء الروم عن الشام وأطرافها وطرد فارس عن حدود السواد وأطراف العراق مع قوتهم وشوكتهم ووفور أموالهم، وانتظام أحوالهم. وفي أيام عمر من فتح جانب المشرق إلى أقصى خراسان ، وقطع دولة العجم وثل عرشهم الراسي البنيان ، الثابث الأركان . ومن ترتيب الأمور، وسياسة الجمهور، وإفاضة العدل، وتقوية الضعفاء، ومن إعراضه من متاع الدنيا وطيباتها وملاذها وشهواتها. وفي أيام عثمان من فتح البلاد وإعلاء لواء الإسلام، وجمع الناس على مصحف واحد مع ما كان له من الورع والتقوى، وتجهيز جيوش المسلمين ، والإنفاق في نصرة الدين والمهاجرة هجرتين، وكونه ختناً للنبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) على ابنتين ، والاستحياء من أدنى شين، وتشرفه بقوله عليه السلام عثمان أخي ورفيقي في الجنة ، وقوله : ألا أستحي ممن تستحي منه ملائكة السماء . وقوله : إنه رجل يدخل الجنة بغير حساب .
As for the indications, what is mutawātir from the days of Abu Bakr: unity of word, harmony of hearts, successive conquests, subduing the people of apostasy, purifying the Arabian Peninsula from polytheism, expelling the Romans from Syria and its borders, driving out the Persians from the borders of the Sawad and the borders of Iraq despite their strength and might and abundance of wealth and order of their affairs. And from the days of 'Umar: conquering the eastern region as far as the farthest Khurasan, ending the empire of the non-Arabs and toppling their firmly established throne of solid foundations; and from the organization of affairs, governance of the populace, spreading justice, strengthening the weak, and his turning away from the goods of this world, its pleasures, delights, and desires. And from the days of 'Uthman: conquering lands, raising high the banner of Islam, uniting the people upon one muṣḥaf along with his piety and God-consciousness, equipping the Muslim armies, spending in support of the religion, emigrating twice (hijratayn), being the son-in-law of the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) to two daughters, being shy of the least shame, being honored by his saying (peace be upon him): 'Uthman is my brother and companion in Paradise,' and his saying: 'Should I not be shy of one whom the angels of heaven are shy of?' and his saying: 'He is a man who will enter Paradise without reckoning.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the 'al-amārāt' (indications/signs) of virtue derived from the historical events during the caliphates of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and 'Uthmān. The author uses these as external evidence (qarā'in) for their superiority, since consensus and direct statements already support it. For Abu Bakr, the signs include: the unification of the community after the death of the Prophet, the defeat of apostasy, and early conquests. For 'Umar, the expansion of the Islamic state to Khurasan, the collapse of the Persian empire, and his just administration and asceticism. For 'Uthmān, the compilation of the Qur'an, his piety, generosity, and the Prophet's commendations. Each set of signs corresponds to the unique challenges and achievements of their respective caliphates.

Key Terms
أمارات
أهل الردة
فتوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as an indication from Abu Bakr's caliphate?
A) Unity of word
B) Harmony of hearts
C) Conquering Khurasan
D) Expelling Romans from Syria

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's caliphate is marked by divinely favored unity, conquest, and purification of Arabia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the list of Abu Bakr's achievements. Which one—unity, harmony, conquest, purification, or expelling enemies—do you feel your community needs most right now? What is one concrete step you can take today to help bring that about?
?
The source says 'purifying the Arabian Peninsula from polytheism'. What false beliefs or attachments in your own life need purification? Will you commit to identifying one today and seeking to remove it?
?
Abu Bakr's leadership brought 'harmony of hearts'. Think of a relationship where there is discord. What can you do, starting tomorrow, to restore harmony? Be specific.

Core Idea
'Umar expanded Islam's reach and established a model of justice.

Sanusi lists extraordinary achievements from 'Umar's reign: the conquest of the East, the collapse of the Persian Empire, and his personal asceticism. These signs point to his high status in Allah's sight.

Indications of 'Uthman's Virtue
Signs from 'Uthman's caliphate as evidence of virtue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical and personal indications from 'Uthman's caliphate that demonstrate his virtue, including the compilation of the Qur'an and the Prophet's commendations.

Core Idea
'Uthman's virtue is marked by piety, generosity, and the Prophet's own praise.

Sanusi presents signs from 'Uthman's caliphate, including the expansion of Islam, the unification of the Qur'anic text, and personal qualities like modesty and the Prophet's honorific statements. These indicate his high rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the indications for 'Uthman
Read the Arabic passage focusing on 'Uthman. Notice the mix of public achievements (conquests, compilation) and personal traits (piety, modesty).
The hadith quotes are especially significant.
2
Examine each Prophetic saying

3
Understand the concept of 'mutawatir' indications
These signs are presented as mutawatir (widely transmitted) to ensure certainty. They supplement consensus and direct texts on 'Uthman's virtue.

Quranic Arguments for 'Ali's Superiority
Sanusi enumerates three Quranic verses used by Shi'a to assert 'Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Quranic verses the Shi'a use to argue for 'Ali's superiority over the other caliphs.

Core Idea
The Shi'a interpret specific Quranic verses as directly referring to 'Ali.

In this passage, the author summarizes the Quranic proof texts that the Shi'a present to support the superiority of 'Ali. These verses include the mubāhala verse, the verse on love for relatives, and the verse on the righteous of the believers. Each is interpreted as pointing to 'Ali's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the main claims. Notice how each verse is cited with a brief interpretive gloss.
2
Identify each verse
List the three verses: the mubāhala verse (3:61), the mawaddah verse (42:23), and the verse on salih al-mu'minin (66:4).
The Shi'a interpret 'ourselves' in 3:61 as 'Ali, and 'the righteous of the believers' as 'Ali.
3
Understand the Shi'a perspective
Ask yourself what these verses are supposed to prove about 'Ali. They are meant to show his elevated status and inclusion in the Prophet's family.
4
Reflect on significance
Why are these verses considered key by the Shi'a? They form the scriptural foundation for the claim of 'Ali's superiority.
Sunni scholars often offer alternative interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تمسكت الشيعة بقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم ) أراد علياً . وقوله تعالى : (قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربي ). وعلي (رضي الله عنه منهم . وقوله تعالى : (وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين). وهو علي . وبقوله : من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه، وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته، فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب . وقوله : أقضاكم علي. وقوله : اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير، فجاء علي. وقوله : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى . إلى غير ذلك. وبأنه أعلم حتى استند رؤساء العلوم إليه، وأخبر بذلك في خبر الوسادة، وأشهد على ما يشهد به غزواته ، حتى قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) «الضربة علي خير من عبادة الثقلين وأزهد حتى طلق الدنيا بكليتها، وأكثر عبادة وسخاوة، وأشرف خلقاً وطلاقة، وأفصح لساناً، وأسبق إسلاماً.
He said: The Shi'a adhered to His saying, the Exalted: {Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves} (3:61) meaning 'Ali. And His saying, the Exalted: {Say: I do not ask you for it any reward except love for the relatives} (42:23); and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is among them. And His saying, the Exalted: {and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers} (66:4); and he is 'Ali. And by his saying: 'Whoever wants to see Adam in his knowledge, and Noah in his piety, and Abraham in his forbearance, and Moses in his awe, and Jesus in his worship, let him look at 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.' And his saying: 'The most knowledgeable among you in judgment is 'Ali.' And his saying: 'O Allah, bring to me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat with me this bird,' and 'Ali came. And his saying: 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses' ... and so on. And because he was the most knowledgeable, to the extent that the leaders of the sciences relied upon him, and he informed of that in the hadith of the pillow, and he witnessed what his battles witness, until the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) said: 'The strike of 'Ali is better than the worship of the two heavy ones (jinn and mankind).' And he was more ascetic, even divorcing this world entirely, and more abundant in worship and generosity, and nobler in character and cheerfulness, and more eloquent in tongue, and foremost in Islam.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage summarizes the arguments that the Shi'a (al-Shīʿa) use to claim the superiority of 'Ali over the other Companions. It begins by citing verses from the Qur'an: the mubāhala verse (Āl 'Imrān 61) where 'the selves' is interpreted as 'Ali; the verse on love for relatives (al-Shūrā 23); and the verse about 'the righteous of the believers' (al-Taḥrīm 4), which they take to refer to 'Ali. Then it lists several hadiths: a composite hadith praising 'Ali as combining the excellences of previous prophets; the hadith 'the most knowledgeable in judgment is 'Ali'; the hadith of the bird; the hadith of the position of Aaron; the hadith that 'Ali's strike is better than worship; and general claims about 'Ali's knowledge, asceticism, worship, generosity, character, eloquence, and precedence in Islam. The passage presents these as the core proof texts of the Shi'a position.

Key Terms
الشيعة (al-Shīʿa)
المباهلة (al-mubāhala)
المودة في القربى (al-mawaddah fī al-qurbā)
صالح المؤمنين (ṣāliḥ al-mu'minīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These Quranic verses are unambiguous proofs of 'Ali's superiority over all other caliphs.
Correction: Sunni scholars often interpret these verses differently, not as explicit references to 'Ali's superiority.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a base a major part of their argument for 'Ali's superiority on specific interpretations of three Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever assume that your own sect's interpretation is the only correct one, without considering that sincere believers from other schools understand the same verses differently? What would it cost you to honestly examine an interpretation that challenges your tradition?
?
These Shia proof texts are deeply cherished by them. Can you think of a verse or teaching in your own tradition that you hold dear, even if others interpret it differently? Does that awareness humble you or harden you?
?
If you were in a debate about 'Ali's superiority and someone cited these verses, would you be able to respond with knowledge rather than dismissal? What would it take for you to actually study the arguments of those you disagree with, not to win, but to understand?

Prophetic Hadiths and Other Evidences for 'Ali's Virtue
Sanusi lists several prophetic hadiths and claims used by Shi'a to prove 'Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the prophetic hadiths and other claims the Shi'a use to argue for 'Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Prophetic traditions are cited to show 'Ali's unique virtues and his status relative to the Prophet.

After Quranic verses, the Shi'a also rely on several well-known hadiths that highlight 'Ali's exceptional qualities. These traditions cover his knowledge, his role as the Prophet's beloved, and his status akin to Aaron. Additional claims about his asceticism and precedence round out the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to note each hadith and claim.
2
Identify key hadiths
List the hadiths: the composite hadith of prophets' excellences, 'most knowledgeable in judgment', the hadith of the bird, the hadith of the position of Aaron, and the hadith of the strike.
The 'hadith of the pillow' is mentioned but not clear.
3
Analyze the themes
The hadiths emphasize 'Ali's comprehensive virtues: knowledge, rank, asceticism, and precedence in Islam.
4
Consider cumulative force
Reflect on how these multiple proofs are intended to establish 'Ali's superiority beyond doubt.
Sunni scholars question the authenticity or interpretation of some of these hadiths.

Redefining Superiority: The Sunni Rebuttal
The author refutes the Shi'a proofs and presents counter-evidence from Sunni sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni response to Shi'a arguments for 'Ali's superiority reframes the discussion around the true meaning of afḍaliyya as greater honor and reward with Allah.

Core Idea
Superiority means greater honor and reward with Allah, not worldly status.

After presenting the Shi'a proofs, the author now delivers the Sunni rebuttal. He clarifies that the debate is about nearness to Allah, not mere worldly merit. He appeals to the consensus of Muslims and even 'Ali's own acknowledgment. This passage sets the foundation for a deeper comparison of the Companions' virtues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's concise style come through.
Notice that the passage is a direct response to previous Shi'a arguments.
2
Identify the definition of afḍaliyya
The author defines superiority as honor (karāmah) with Allah and abundance of reward (thawāb). This is the key framing.
This redefinition is the core of the Sunni rebuttal.
3
Notice the sources of evidence
The author cites the general consensus of Muslims and 'Ali's own testimony. This grounds the response in accepted authority.
Think about why citing 'Ali himself is rhetorically powerful.
4
Reflect on the hinted counter-evidence
The passage ends by referencing 'precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners.' This alludes to the virtues of Abu Bakr and 'Umar.
This hint will be unpacked in subsequent lessons.
5
Consider the overall argument
Ask yourself: How does redefining superiority change the debate? Why is the focus on honor with Allah important?
This connects to the broader Sunni doctrine of the Companions' ranking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أن الكلام في الأفضلية بمعنى الكرامة عند الله، وكثرة الثواب، وقد شهد في ذلك عامة المسلمين، واعترف علي رضي الله عنه به . وعارض ما ذكرتم ما ذكرنا مع أن فيه مواضع بحث لا تخفى، سيما حديث سبق الإسلام والسيف في إعلاء الأعلام.
The answer is that the discussion about superiority is in the sense of honor (karāmah) with Allah and abundance of reward (thawāb). The general body of Muslims have testified to that, and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) acknowledged it. What you have mentioned is countered by what we have mentioned, although in it are points of investigation not hidden, especially the hadith of precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage gives the Sunni response to the Shi'a proofs for 'Ali's superiority. It begins by clarifying the nature of afḍaliyya: it means greater honor and reward with Allah, not merely worldly status. The response states that the consensus (or overwhelming majority) of Muslims has attested to the order of virtue (Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthmān), and that 'Ali himself acknowledged this (as shown in the earlier reports). The author then claims that the Shi'a proofs are countered by Sunni evidences, and he hints at several points of critical discussion, especially the hadith about 'precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners'—likely a reference to the virtues of Abu Bakr's early conversion and 'Umar's role in spreading Islam by force. This sets the stage for a detailed comparison.

Key Terms
أفضلية (afḍaliyya)
كرامة (karāmah)
ثواب (thawāb)
سبق الإسلام (sabaq al-Islām)
السيف (al-sayf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Superiority means being better in worldly conquests, political leadership, or personal achievements.
Correction: The text explicitly states that superiority (afḍaliyya) is about honor with Allah and abundance of reward, not worldly criteria.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response to Shi'a arguments for 'Ali's superiority centers on redefining superiority as nearness to Allah, citing consensus and specific hadiths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The author frames the debate by citing 'Ali's own acknowledgment. Is there a belief you hold about a revered figure that you would be willing to reexamine if evidence showed that figure actually held a different view?
?
The passage defines true superiority as honor and reward with Allah. If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow, would your deeds reflect that you genuinely sought His honor over the approval of people? What is one thing you can change today to align your actions with this truth?

The Quranic Proof for 'Ali's Superiority: The Mubāhala Verse
Enumerates three sources and begins Quranic quotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of 'Ali's superiority begin their case by citing the mubāhala verse as a Quranic proof, and the significance of the word 'ourselves' in that argument.

Core Idea
The mubāhala verse is a key Quranic text used to argue for 'Ali's superiority.

This lesson introduces the arguments of those who maintain that 'Ali (ra) is superior to the other Companions. They rely on three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunna, and reason. The Qur'anic proof they cite is the mubāhala verse (Āl 'Imrān 61), which they interpret as including 'Ali among 'ourselves' (anfusanā), indicating his high rank. The passage here sets up their position, though the full argument continues beyond this page.

The Mubahala Proof for Ali's Superiority
Presents and explains the mubahala verse as proof for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse of mubahala is used to argue for Ali's superiority over other Companions.

Core Idea
The Prophet included Ali as 'himself' in the mubahala, implying his unique status.

The first Quranic proof for Ali's superiority comes from the mubahala verse (3:61). The author explains how the Prophet's inclusion of Ali in 'ourselves' indicates his exceptional rank. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the mubahala proof passage
Read the Arabic text and translation of the section discussing Surah Al-Imran 3:61. Notice how the author interprets 'ourselves' as referring to Ali.
The verse is about invoking a curse with the Christians of Najran.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that since the Prophet only brought Ali from his male cousins, and called him 'our selves', Ali must be like the Prophet's own self, hence superior.
3
Reflect on the context
Remember that this argument is presented as part of a larger discussion; the author will later refute it. This lesson only covers the claim.
The passage is from a Sunni theological work, not an endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim the superiority (afdaliyya) of Ali ibn Abi Talib over other Companions, drawing on the Quran and Sunna. It is structured as a list of proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surah Al 'Imran (3:61) about the mubahala (invoking curse) with the Christians of Najran. The author explains that the Prophet used 'ourselves' (anfusana) including Ali, and only Ali from his male cousins accompanied him, implying Ali is like the Prophet's own self, hence superior. Second, the verse from Surah Ash-Shura (42:23) commanding love of relatives (al-qurba); Saeed ibn Jubayr interprets the relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons, and such obligatory love indicates superiority. Third, the verse from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4) where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned; Ibn Abbas identifies the righteous believers as Ali, so his support for the Prophet is paired with divine aid, showing his status. Then the passage moves to hadiths: (1) The Prophet likening Ali to Adam in knowledge, Noah in piety, Abraham in forbearance, Moses in majesty, and Jesus in worship—whoever equals these prophets is clearly superior. (2) The saying 'The most judicious among you is Ali' (aqdakum Ali) implying completeness and knowledge. (3) The prayer that God bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali comes. (4) The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' indicating Ali's closeness and status, as Aaron was Moses' brother and supporter. (5) The hadith 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla' (from Ghadir Khumm) implying leadership. (6) The promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (who turned out to be Ali). The passage ends mid-sentence on page 296.

Key Terms
المباهلة (mubahala)
أفضلية (superiority)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mubahala verse definitively proves Ali's superiority over all Companions.
Correction: In the context of Sharh al-Maqasid, this argument is presented to be refuted later; the author does not endorse it as conclusive.

Checkpoint
Which event is the basis for the argument that Ali is like the Prophet's 'self'?
A) The Battle of Badr
B) The mubahala with Christians of Najran
C) The conquest of Khaybar
D) The incident of Ghadir Khumm

Key Takeaway
The mubahala verse is used to argue that Ali holds a status equivalent to the Prophet's own self, implying superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Ali is truly in the position of the Prophet's own self, then his example carries prophetic weight. Are you honestly following Ali's example in your daily life? Name one area where you fall short and commit to change.
?
The mubahala involved invoking a curse on the wrongdoer. If you were engaged in a religious dispute, would you be willing to call upon Allah to curse you if you were wrong? What does your hesitation reveal about your certainty in your own beliefs?
?
This lesson interprets a Quranic verse in a way that challenges common assumptions. How do you ensure your own interpretations of the Quran are not shaped by sect or desire rather than truth? What concrete step will you take this week to guard against bias?

The Love of Relatives Proof
Uses Quran 42:23 and its interpretation to argue for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command to love the relatives (qurba) is used to argue for Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Obligatory love for Ali and his family implies their superiority over others.

The second Quranic proof is based on the verse 'Say: I do not ask you for any recompense except love of the relatives' (42:23). The author cites an interpretation that identifies these relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons. Let's see how this leads to the claim of superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation for the section on Surah Ash-Shura 42:23. Note the explanation by Sa'id ibn Jubayr identifying the relatives.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The author argues that if love for these individuals is made obligatory by the Quran, then they must be superior, as obligatory love implies high status.
3
Consider the context
Again, remember that this is a presentation of an argument that will be examined critically later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'

?

?

?
The Prophet identified the relatives as only four individuals. Does your love for the Ahl al-Bayt lead you to honor and follow them above others, or is it just a feeling without commitment?

The Righteous Believers Proof
Uses Quran 66:4 interpreted as referring to Ali to argue for his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic phrase 'righteous believers' is interpreted to support Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Ali is named alongside God and Gabriel as a supporter of the Prophet, indicating his high rank.

The third Quranic proof comes from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4), where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned as supporters of the Prophet. Ibn 'Abbas identifies the 'righteous believers' as Ali, thereby linking him directly with divine support. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation for the section on Surah At-Tahrim 66:4. Note the interpretation by Ibn 'Abbas.
2
Understand the argument
The author argues that being mentioned alongside God and Gabriel in support of the Prophet shows Ali's supreme status, implying he is the best of believers.
3
Reflect on the context
This is another argument that will be critiqued later in the larger discussion.

Hadith Proofs: Equality with Prophets and Aqdakum
Presents two hadiths as evidence for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two hadiths—comparing Ali to prophets and calling him 'most judicious'—are used to support his superiority.

Core Idea
If Ali equals prophets in perfections, he is superior; being 'most judicious' implies completeness.

The first two hadiths presented are: (1) The Prophet said whoever wants to see the virtues of various prophets should look at Ali, and (2) 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' These are used to claim that Ali combines the perfections of multiple prophets and possesses superior judgment. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths
Read the Arabic and translation for the two hadiths: the one comparing Ali to Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus, and the 'aqdakum' hadith.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that resemblance to prophets in their distinctive qualities indicates superiority, and being 'most judicious' (aqdak) implies superior knowledge and completeness.
3
Reflect on the chain
These arguments are presented as part of a broader discussion; later the author will evaluate their strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim the superiority (afdaliyya) of Ali ibn Abi Talib over other Companions, drawing on the Quran and Sunna. It is structured as a list of proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surah Al 'Imran (3:61) about the mubahala (invoking curse) with the Christians of Najran. The author explains that the Prophet used 'ourselves' (anfusana) including Ali, and only Ali from his male cousins accompanied him, implying Ali is like the Prophet's own self, hence superior. Second, the verse from Surah Ash-Shura (42:23) commanding love of relatives (al-qurba); Saeed ibn Jubayr interprets the relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons, and such obligatory love indicates superiority. Third, the verse from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4) where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned; Ibn Abbas identifies the righteous believers as Ali, so his support for the Prophet is paired with divine aid, showing his status. Then the passage moves to hadiths: (1) The Prophet likening Ali to Adam in knowledge, Noah in piety, Abraham in forbearance, Moses in majesty, and Jesus in worship—whoever equals these prophets is clearly superior. (2) The saying 'The most judicious among you is Ali' (aqdakum Ali) implying completeness and knowledge. (3) The prayer that God bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali comes. (4) The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' indicating Ali's closeness and status, as Aaron was Moses' brother and supporter. (5) The hadith 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla' (from Ghadir Khumm) implying leadership. (6) The promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (who turned out to be Ali). The passage ends mid-sentence on page 296.

Key Terms
أقضاكم (most judicious)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about Ali's resemblance to prophets is universally accepted as authentic and proof of his superiority.
Correction: Scholars debate the authenticity and interpretation of this hadith; it is not considered definitive by all.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet say about Ali in the first hadith in this passage?
A) Ali is the best of the Companions
B) Whoever wishes to see the virtues of certain prophets should look at Ali
C) Ali is the most knowledgeable
D) Ali will be the leader of the Muslims after him

Key Takeaway
These hadiths are used to argue that Ali surpasses others by combining prophetic virtues and possessing superior judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the prophetic virtues—knowledge, piety, forbearance, majesty, or worship—do you find most lacking in your own character? What is one practical step you can take today to strengthen that virtue, following Ali's example?
?
The hadith calls Ali the 'most judicious'—a wisdom rooted in completeness and knowledge. Think of a recent decision you made. Was it driven by haste or worldly concerns? How might you cultivate the same depth of judgment in your choices from now on?
?
If Ali embodies the best of each prophet, then our own pursuit of perfection is not about achieving greatness for its own sake, but about closeness to Allah. What is the one character flaw you have been excusing that prevents you from drawing closer to Him? Will you commit today to uprooting it?

Hadith Proofs: Most Beloved and Harun Comparison
Presents two hadiths about Ali's status as evidence for his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadiths about Ali being the most beloved to God and his being like Aaron to Moses are used to argue for his superiority.

Core Idea
Being most beloved to God implies greater reward and superiority; Aaron's status with Moses shows Ali's high rank.

The next two hadiths in the list are: (1) The Prophet prayed for the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali came; (2) The Prophet said to Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses.' Both are used to demonstrate Ali's special closeness to God and the Prophet, implying his superiority over all others.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths
Read the Arabic and translation for the two hadiths: the 'most beloved' hadith and the 'Harun' hadith.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that being the most beloved to God entails greater reward and thus superiority. The Harun hadith is used to show that Ali is to the Prophet as Aaron was to Moses – there was no one better than Aaron in Moses' time.
3
Note the logic
The author explicitly says 'the most beloved to God is greater in reward, which is the meaning of superiority.' For the Harun hadith, similarly, no one was better than Aaron, so Ali is best.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If being most beloved to God means greater reward, what does your daily life reveal about your pursuit of being among the most beloved? Are you actively seeking actions that increase your love for Him, or are you satisfied with mediocrity?
?
The Prophet placed Ali in the same rank as Aaron was to Moses. Do you have anyone in your life with whom you share such a deep bond of faith? Are you that person for someone else? What step can you take today to strengthen your ties with the righteous?
?
The passage teaches that being more beloved implies superiority in God's sight. In your scale of values, what are you truly trying to be superior in? How can you realign your goals to prioritize what brings you closer to being among the most beloved to Allah?
