The passage then shifts to a methodological ruling: the scholars (المحققون) have concluded that investigating the details of disputes among the Companions is not part of Islamic creed (عقائد دينية) or kalam principles (قواعد كلامية). Such investigation has no religious benefit and may even harm one's certainty. Nonetheless, they mention a little of it for two reasons, which are not given here as the text breaks off. This highlights the Sunni approach of avoiding excessive debate about Companions' conflicts, while providing necessary justifications to protect their honor.

Key Terms
المحققين
العقائد الدينية
القواعد الكلامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is obligatory or recommended to study every detail of conflicts among the Sahaba to fully understand Islamic history.
Correction: The passage rules that such research is not part of religious beliefs and may harm one's faith, implying it is not recommended.

Checkpoint
According to the verifying scholars, what is the ruling on researching the disputes among the Companions?
A) It is obligatory for every Muslim.
B) It is part of theological principles (قواعد كلامية).
C) It is not from religious beliefs and may harm certainty.
D) It is recommended to understand history.

Key Takeaway
The details of Sahaba disputes are not part of Islamic dogma and are better left undiscussed to protect faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever participated in or listened to a discussion about the disagreements among the Companions? How did that affect your respect and love for them? What will you do the next time such a topic comes up?
?
This lesson warns that delving into these issues can harm your certainty (اليقين). Is there any doubt or confusion about any Companion that still lingers in your heart due to something you've heard? Commit today to seeking only that which strengthens your faith and protects your yaqeen.
?
The scholars avoided unnecessary debate about the Sahaba because it brings no benefit and can damage imaan. Think of one other matter you spend time on that offers no religious benefit and might weaken your belief. What concrete step will you take this week to replace it with something beneficial?

The Prophet's Command to Love His Companions and Refuting Fabrications
Hadith warning against hating Companions and refuting Rafidah fabrications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic imperative to love the Companions and the evidence that Rafidah fabrications about them are historically baseless.

Core Idea
Loving the Companions is loving the Prophet; hating them is hating him.

This lesson presents a powerful hadith in which the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) emphatically commands respect for his Companions. It then addresses the fabricated stories spread by extremist Rafidah groups, showing that these slanders were unknown in the early centuries and even absent from the teachings of Ahl al-Bayt. The passage invites the reader to hold fast to the straight path and reject such hearsay.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic hadith carefully. Notice the repeated phrase 'الله الله في أصحابي' which shows the Prophet's intense emphasis.
The double repetition indicates the seriousness of the command.
2
Understand the logical implication
The hadith states that love for the Companions stems from love for the Prophet, and hatred for them stems from hatred for him. This makes the Companions a touchstone of one's faith.
3
Identify the target of the warning
The author then warns against the Rawafid, especially the extremists (ghulat), who exaggerate in hating some Companions and slander them based on fabricated stories.
Note the phrase 'بناء على حكايات وافتراءات' indicating these are mere tales and falsehoods.
4
Examine the author's evidence
The author provides a key proof: these stories were not present in the second and third centuries nor among the pure Progeny (Ahl al-Bayt). In fact, the Ahl al-Bayt praised the Companions in their sermons, letters, and poetry.
This historical argument is central to refuting the Rafidah claims.
5
Reflect on the practical takeaway
The author advises not to listen to such tales, as they mislead the youth and perplex the middle-aged. The lesson ends with reliance on Allah as the Guide.
Consider how this warning applies to contemporary discussions about the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «الله الله في أصحابي الله الله في أصحابي، لا تتخذوهم غرضاً من بعدي، فمن أحبهم فبحبي أحبهم، ومن أبغضهم فببغضي أبغضهم . وللروافض سيما الغلاة منهم مبالغات في بغض البعض من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم والطعن فيهم بناء على حكايات وافتراءات لم تكن في القرن الثاني والثالث . فإياك والإصغاء إليها . فإنها تضل الأحداث، وتحير الأوساط، وإن كانت لا تؤثر فيمن له استقامة على الصراط المستقيم . وكفاك شاهداً على ما ذكرنا أنها لم تكن في القرون السالفة، ولا فيما بين العترة الطاهرة بل ثناؤهم على عظماء الصحابة وعلماء السنة والجماعة، والمهديين من مشهور وفي خطبهم ورسائلهم وأشعارهم ومدائحهم مذكور. والله الهادي .
And the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: 'Allah, Allah regarding my Companions! Allah, Allah regarding my Companions! Do not make them a target after me. Whoever loves them loves me because of my love, and whoever hates them hates me because of my hatred.' And the Rawafid, especially the extremists among them, have exaggerations in hating some of the Companions (رضي الله عنهم) and slandering them based on tales and fabrications that did not exist in the second and third centuries. So beware of listening to them. Verily, they mislead the youth and perplex the middle-aged, although they do not affect those who have uprightness on the straight path. And sufficient for you as evidence of what we have mentioned is that they did not exist in the past centuries nor among the pure Progeny; rather, their praise of the great Companions and the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah and the guided ones is well-known, and it is mentioned in their sermons, letters, poems, and eulogies. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage begins by citing a hadith in which the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) emphatically commands respect for his Companions, twice saying 'Allah, Allah regarding my Companions.' The hadith warns against making them a target and states that love for them is a sign of love for the Prophet, while hatred for them is a sign of hatred for him. The author then applies this to the Rafidah, especially their extremists (ghulat), who exaggerate in hating some Companions and defame them based on fabricated stories that did not circulate in the early Islamic centuries (second and third). The author warns his reader not to listen to such tales, as they lead astray the young and confuse the mature, though they do not affect those firmly on the straight path. The proof that these stories are fabrications is that they were absent from earlier generations, including the 'pure Progeny' (al-'itrah al-tahirah), i.e., the Ahl al-Bayt, who actually praised the great Companions and scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah. Their praises are attested in their sermons, letters, poems, and eulogies. The author ends with a note that Allah is the Guide.

Key Terms
الصحابة
الروافض
الغلاة
العترة الطاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Rafidah criticisms of the Companions are historically valid and go back to the early Islamic period.
Correction: The author shows these criticisms are late fabrications, not present in the second/third centuries nor among Ahl al-Bayt, who actually praised the Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence that the Rafidah's stories against the Companions are fabrications?
A) They were mentioned in Sunni books.
B) They did not exist in the second and third centuries nor among the Ahl al-Bayt.
C) The Ahl al-Bayt also criticized the Companions.
D) The Quran explicitly prohibits such talk.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) made love of his Companions a test of love for him, and any criticism of them that is absent from the early Islamic sources and from the teachings of Ahl al-Bayt is a fabrication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly love all the Companions of the Prophet as a sign of your love for him, or have you allowed any accusation or doubt about any of them to linger in your heart? Examine your heart honestly: is there any resentment or criticism you've internalized from modern anti-Sahaba narratives?
?
The author warns that listening to fabrications about the Companions misleads youth and confuses the mature. Have you ever uncritically accepted a story about a Companion without checking its source against early Islamic evidence? Will you commit to verifying every such tale using the standard the author gives—absence from early centuries and contradiction with Ahl al-Bayt's praise?
?
Consider the evidence: these fabrications were unknown in the second and third centuries, and even the Prophet's own household praised the Companions. If you have ever entertained a negative view of a Companion, can you now reject it as a fabrication, not out of blind loyalty, but because of the clear proof from history and from Ahl al-Bayt themselves? What will you do to actively correct such views in your own mind and among those you influence?

Ali's Delay in Pledging to Abu Bakr
Sanusi explains Ali's delay in pledging to Abu Bakr due to grief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali delayed his pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr, as explained by Sunni scholars, and how his grief prevented immediate ijtihad.

Core Idea
Ali's grief for the Prophet delayed his allegiance, not opposition.

Sunni theology defends Ali's initial hesitation in pledging to Abu Bakr by attributing it to overwhelming grief after the Prophet's death. This lesson explains the principle of 'udhr bil-'uzr (excuse due to incapacity) in early Islamic political thought. You will see how Ali later joined the community once the truth became clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ali and Abu Bakr
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the causal link between grief and lack of ijtihad.
The key phrase is 'lam yatafarragh li-l-nazar wa-l-ijtihad'.
2
Understand the role of 'udhr
Consider how grief is presented as a legitimate excuse for delay. Contrast with the later action when he reflected and accepted.
3
Reflect on sectarian implications
Note that this explanation counters any suggestion that Ali opposed Abu Bakr's caliphate. It shows he eventually agreed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول أما توقف علي رضي الله عنه في بيعة أبي بكر رضي الله تعالى عنه فيحمل على أنه لما أصابه من الكآبة والحزن بفقد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يتفرغ للنظر والاجتهاد. فلما نظر وظهر له الحق، دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة. وأما توقفه عن نصرة عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنه ودفع الغوغاء عنه فلأنه لم يأذن في ذلك وكان يتجافى عن الحرب وإراقة الدماء، حتى قال : من وضع السلاح من غلماني فهو حر. ومع ذلك فقد دفع عنه الحسنان رضي الله عنهما ولم ينفع، وكان ما كان ولم يكن رضا من علي رضي الله عنه بذلك وإعانة عليه. ولهذا قال رضي الله عنه : والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات عليه. وتوقف في قبول البيعة إعظاماً لقتل عثمان وإنكاراً، وكذا طلحة والزبير. إلا أن من حضر من وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار أقسموا عليه وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمة وصيانة دار الهجرة، إذ قتلة عثمان قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة، والفتك بأهلها. وكانوا جهلة لا سابقة لهم في الإسلام، ولا علم لهم بأمر الدين، ولا صحبة مع الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فقبل البيعة. وتوقفه عن قصاص قتلة عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنه إما لشوكتهم وكثرتهم وقوتهم وحرصهم بالخروج على من يطالبهم بدمه فاقتضى النظر الصائب تأخير الأمر احترازاً عن إثارة الفتنة وإما لأنه رأى أنهم بغاة لما لهم من المنعة الظاهرة والتأويل.
So we say: As for 'Ali's (رضي الله عنه) hesitation in the pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr (رضي الله تعالى عنه), it is attributed to the fact that when he was afflicted by grief and sorrow at the loss of the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم), he was not free to engage in reflection and ijtihad. Then when he reflected and the truth became manifest to him, he entered into what the community entered into. And as for his hesitation in supporting 'Uthman (رضي الله تعالى عنه) and repelling the rabble from him, it is because he did not authorize that, and he avoided war and the spilling of blood, to the extent that he said: 'Whoever of my slaves lays down his weapon is free.' Nevertheless, he did send al-Hasan and al-Husayn (رضي الله عنهما) to defend him, but it did not avail, and what happened happened; and it was not a matter of 'Ali (رضي الله عنه) being pleased with that or assisting in it. For this reason he said (رضي الله عنه): 'By Allah, I did not kill 'Uthman, nor did I assist (malatu) against him.' And he hesitated to accept the pledge of allegiance out of reverence for the killing of 'Uthman and as a disapproval, and likewise Talhah and al-Zubayr. But those present among the eminent Muhajirun and Ansar imposed an oath upon him and adjured him by Allah to preserve the rest of the Ummah and protect the abode of the Hijrah, for the killers of 'Uthman intended to seize Medina and slaughter its people. They were ignorant people with no prior standing in Islam, no knowledge of the religion, and no companionship with the Messenger (صلى الله عليه وسلم). So he accepted the pledge. And his hesitation concerning the retaliation (qisas) against the killers of 'Uthman (رضي الله تعالى عنه) was either due to their strength (shawkah), large number, and power, and their eagerness to rebel against anyone who demanded his blood, so sound judgment required delaying the matter to avoid igniting civil strife (fitnah), or because he considered them to be rebels (bughat) due to their manifest might and interpretation (ta'wil).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage provides four explanations for 'Ali's (RA) delays in critical events, introduced by the phrase 'we say' (fa-naqul). First, his delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr was due to his deep grief over the Prophet's death, which prevented him from immediate ijtihad; once he examined the situation, he recognized the truth and joined the community. Second, his hesitation to support 'Uthman against the rebels was because 'Uthman did not request his aid, and 'Ali generally avoided war and bloodshed—he even freed any slave who laid down arms. He did send his sons Hasan and Husayn to defend 'Uthman, but it did not succeed. 'Ali explicitly denied having killed 'Uthman or assisted his killers. Third, his delay in accepting the caliphate after 'Uthman's murder was out of reverence for the grave event and his disapproval of the bloodshed; Talhah and al-Zubayr initially shared this hesitation. However, the senior Muhajirun and Ansar insisted, warning that the murderers intended to seize Medina and kill its people, and since the killers were ignorant with no standing in Islam or companionship with the Prophet, 'Ali finally accepted. Fourth, his postponement of prosecuting 'Uthman's killers was due to their power and numbers and the risk of civil war (fitnah), or because he considered them rebels (bughat) due to their strength and adherence to a (flawed) interpretation.

Key Terms
بيعة أبي بكر
النظر والاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali delayed pledging because he opposed Abu Bakr's caliphate on principle.
Correction: The passage says his delay was due to grief, not opposition; he later joined the community after reflection.

Checkpoint
What was the reason given for Ali's delay in pledging to Abu Bakr?
A) He disagreed with Abu Bakr's leadership.
B) He was occupied with grief and sorrow over the Prophet's death.
C) He was waiting for more companions to arrive.
D) He was ill.

Key Takeaway
Ali's delay in pledging to Abu Bakr was due to overwhelming grief, not opposition, and he later affirmed Abu Bakr's caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has grief or sorrow prevented you from facing a truth you already knew? What decision are you postponing today because of emotional pain, and when will you commit to reflection and action?
?
Ali's hesitation was not opposition but a natural human reaction. In your own life, where have you assumed someone's delay meant rejection, only to later learn it was due to personal struggle? How can you extend that same charity of interpretation to others in your community?

Ali's Hesitation to Support Uthman
Sanusi justifies Ali's lack of military support for Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali did not actively fight to defend Uthman during the siege, as explained by his aversion to war and Uthman's own request.

Core Idea
Ali avoided bloodshed, not because he approved of Uthman's murder.

Ali's reluctance to support Uthman militarily is often misunderstood as complicity. This lesson reveals that Uthman did not ask for help, and Ali's general policy was to avoid war. He even sent his sons but it was insufficient. The passage clears Ali of any blame.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ali and Uthman
Identify the three key points: Uthman did not authorize support, Ali hated war, and he attempted some defense but it failed.
2
Analyze Ali's statement
Look at the quote: 'By Allah, I did not kill Uthman, nor did I assist against him.' Understand how this counters accusations.
3
Connect to broader theme
Notice the principle: not fighting does not imply approval. Ali's action (or lack thereof) was based on prudence and respect for Uthman's wishes.

Source Text

Key Takeaway
Ali's hesitation to support Uthman was due to Uthman's own decision and Ali's aversion to war, not approval of the murder.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you remained silent or inactive in a conflict because you believed it was the right thing, yet others misunderstood your motives. How does Ali's example of clear denial and explanation challenge you to defend your intentions even when your actions are misread?
?
Ali sent his sons to help Uthman, but it was futile. When you see someone suffering, do you assume your efforts must succeed to be worthwhile? What does this lesson teach you about the sincerity of trying even when outcomes are beyond your control?
?
Ali said, 'By Allah, I did not kill Uthman nor assist against him.' Are there any sins or failures that others have attributed to you where you need to make a clear declaration of innocence? Are you willing to stand before Allah with the same clarity about your intentions and actions?

Ali's Delay in Accepting the Caliphate
Sanusi explains Ali's initial refusal and eventual acceptance of caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali initially hesitated to accept the caliphate after Uthman's murder, and what compelled him to eventually accept.

Core Idea
Ali's initial refusal was out of reverence for Uthman's blood; he accepted only to save Medina.

After Uthman's murder, Ali was asked to become caliph but hesitated. This lesson explains his hesitation as a sign of respect for the tragedy and his eventual acceptance due to the pressing danger from the murderers. It shows that Ali's rule was a reluctant duty, not an ambitious grab.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on caliphate acceptance
Note the phrase 'i'zaman li-qatl 'Uthman wa inkar' — reverence and disapproval. See that Talhah and al-Zubayr also hesitated.
2
Understand the pressure from Muhajirun and Ansar
They adjured Ali to accept to prevent chaos. The killers were ignorant outsiders threatening Medina.
3
Reflect on the theme of necessity
Ali accepted only when the community insisted and the danger became clear. This counters claims he sought power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول أما توقف علي رضي الله عنه في بيعة أبي بكر رضي الله تعالى عنه فيحمل على أنه لما أصابه من الكآبة والحزن بفقد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يتفرغ للنظر والاجتهاد. فلما نظر وظهر له الحق، دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة. وأما توقفه عن نصرة عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنه ودفع الغوغاء عنه فلأنه لم يأذن في ذلك وكان يتجافى عن الحرب وإراقة الدماء، حتى قال : من وضع السلاح من غلماني فهو حر. ومع ذلك فقد دفع عنه الحسنان رضي الله عنهما ولم ينفع، وكان ما كان ولم يكن رضا من علي رضي الله عنه بذلك وإعانة عليه. ولهذا قال رضي الله عنه : والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات عليه. وتوقف في قبول البيعة إعظاماً لقتل عثمان وإنكاراً، وكذا طلحة والزبير. إلا أن من حضر من وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار أقسموا عليه وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمة وصيانة دار الهجرة، إذ قتلة عثمان قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة، والفتك بأهلها. وكانوا جهلة لا سابقة لهم في الإسلام، ولا علم لهم بأمر الدين، ولا صحبة مع الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فقبل البيعة. وتوقفه عن قصاص قتلة عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنه إما لشوكتهم وكثرتهم وقوتهم وحرصهم بالخروج على من يطالبهم بدمه فاقتضى النظر الصائب تأخير الأمر احترازاً عن إثارة الفتنة وإما لأنه رأى أنهم بغاة لما لهم من المنعة الظاهرة والتأويل.
So we say: As for 'Ali's (رضي الله عنه) hesitation in the pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr (رضي الله تعالى عنه), it is attributed to the fact that when he was afflicted by grief and sorrow at the loss of the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم), he was not free to engage in reflection and ijtihad. Then when he reflected and the truth became manifest to him, he entered into what the community entered into. And as for his hesitation in supporting 'Uthman (رضي الله تعالى عنه) and repelling the rabble from him, it is because he did not authorize that, and he avoided war and the spilling of blood, to the extent that he said: 'Whoever of my slaves lays down his weapon is free.' Nevertheless, he did send al-Hasan and al-Husayn (رضي الله عنهما) to defend him, but it did not avail, and what happened happened; and it was not a matter of 'Ali (رضي الله عنه) being pleased with that or assisting in it. For this reason he said (رضي الله عنه): 'By Allah, I did not kill 'Uthman, nor did I assist (malatu) against him.' And he hesitated to accept the pledge of allegiance out of reverence for the killing of 'Uthman and as a disapproval, and likewise Talhah and al-Zubayr. But those present among the eminent Muhajirun and Ansar imposed an oath upon him and adjured him by Allah to preserve the rest of the Ummah and protect the abode of the Hijrah, for the killers of 'Uthman intended to seize Medina and slaughter its people. They were ignorant people with no prior standing in Islam, no knowledge of the religion, and no companionship with the Messenger (صلى الله عليه وسلم). So he accepted the pledge. And his hesitation concerning the retaliation (qisas) against the killers of 'Uthman (رضي الله تعالى عنه) was either due to their strength (shawkah), large number, and power, and their eagerness to rebel against anyone who demanded his blood, so sound judgment required delaying the matter to avoid igniting civil strife (fitnah), or because he considered them to be rebels (bughat) due to their manifest might and interpretation (ta'wil).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage provides four explanations for 'Ali's (RA) delays in critical events, introduced by the phrase 'we say' (fa-naqul). First, his delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr was due to his deep grief over the Prophet's death, which prevented him from immediate ijtihad; once he examined the situation, he recognized the truth and joined the community. Second, his hesitation to support 'Uthman against the rebels was because 'Uthman did not request his aid, and 'Ali generally avoided war and bloodshed—he even freed any slave who laid down arms. He did send his sons Hasan and Husayn to defend 'Uthman, but it did not succeed. 'Ali explicitly denied having killed 'Uthman or assisted his killers. Third, his delay in accepting the caliphate after 'Uthman's murder was out of reverence for the grave event and his disapproval of the bloodshed; Talhah and al-Zubayr initially shared this hesitation. However, the senior Muhajirun and Ansar insisted, warning that the murderers intended to seize Medina and kill its people, and since the killers were ignorant with no standing in Islam or companionship with the Prophet, 'Ali finally accepted. Fourth, his postponement of prosecuting 'Uthman's killers was due to their power and numbers and the risk of civil war (fitnah), or because he considered them rebels (bughat) due to their strength and adherence to a (flawed) interpretation.

Key Terms
قبول البيعة
المهاجرين والأنصار
دار الهجرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali eagerly sought the caliphate after Uthman's murder.
Correction: He hesitated out of reverence and only accepted under duress to protect Medina.

Checkpoint
Why did Ali finally accept the caliphate?
A) He wanted power.
B) The Muhajirun and Ansar forced him, warning that the killers would destroy Medina.
C) Talhah and al-Zubayr convinced him.
D) He had a divine command.

Key Takeaway
Ali accepted the caliphate reluctantly, out of reverence for Uthman's blood and only to prevent the killers from seizing Medina.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Would you be willing to take on a position of leadership or responsibility in your community even if it meant personal sacrifice, just to prevent greater harm? What would hold you back?
?
Ali's reverence for the sanctity of 'Uthman's blood stopped him from accepting the caliphate. How deeply do you honor the lives and dignity of Muslims today, especially when their blood is shed? Is there any area of your life where you have become desensitized to the suffering of others?
?
The killers were described as ignorant with no standing in Islam. When you see people causing fitna in the Muslim community, do you view them with contempt or with a sense of responsibility to guide and protect? What action will you take this week to fulfill your duty toward the Ummah?

Ali's Delay in Qisas for Uthman's Killers
Sanusi provides two justifications for Ali's delay in qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible reasons for Ali's postponement of retaliation against Uthman's killers, as presented by Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
Ali delayed qisas to avoid civil war or because he deemed them rebels with interpretation.

One of the most controversial decisions of Ali's caliphate was his delay in prosecuting Uthman's murderers. This lesson explains two plausible justifications: either he feared their military strength would cause fitna, or he considered them bughat (rebels) whose case required calm resolution. The passage shows Ali's prudence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on qisas
Notice the two alternatives introduced by 'imma... wa-imma'. Understand each reasoning.
2
First alternative: fear of fitna
The killers were powerful and numerous; attacking them could spark widespread civil war.
3
Second alternative: bughat status
Ali may have considered them rebels due to their strength and ta'wil (interpretation), so immediate qisas was not appropriate.
4
Reflect on political implications
This passage defends Ali's decision as wise and Islamicly grounded, not as weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Two Benefits of Discussing the Early Fitnah
Author enumerates two benefits of discussing fitnah and cites Abu Hanifah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key benefits of studying the early fitnah disputes: protecting sound intellects from false beliefs and deriving juridical rulings for rebels.

Core Idea
Discussing fitnah safeguards intellect and provides legal guidance.

The author opens by stating two important reasons why it is worthwhile to examine the early conflicts among the Companions. These benefits are both theological and legal. Instead of avoiding sensitive topics, he argues that proper discussion yields protection from corrupt beliefs and practical rulings in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two benefits and the supporting quote from Abu Hanifah.
The passage is concise; the benefits are stated in the first few lines.
2
Identify the two benefits
Find the first benefit: protecting sound minds from vile beliefs spread by some narrators. Find the second: providing legal rulings for the chapter on rebels (bughat).
The second benefit is linked to Abu Hanifah's reliance on Ali's example.
3
Understand the legal implication
Notice that without Ali's conduct regarding the Khawarij, Abu Hanifah says we would not know the proper sirah (way) to deal with rebels. This shows the practical importance of studying historical disputes.
This connects the benefit to Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما صون الأذهان السليمة عن التدنس بالعقائد الردية التي توقعها حكايات بعض الروافض ورواياتهم. وثانيها ابتناء بعض الأحكام الفقهية في باب البغاة عليها. إذ ليس في ذلك نصوص يرجع إليها، ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة (۱) لولا علي لم نكن نعرف السيرة في الخوارج وكان النبي خص عليا بتعليم تلك الأحكام لما علم من اختصاصه بالحاجة إليها أو علمها غيره أيضاً، لكنهم لم يحتاجوا إلى البيان والتبليغ لما رأوا من معاملة علي على وفقها من غير تغيير. فنقول: أما توقف علي في بيعة أبي بكر فيحمل على أنه لما أصابه من الكآبة والحزن بفقد رسول الله لم يتفرغ للنظر والاجتهاد. فلما نظر وظهر له الحق، دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة. وأما توقفه عن نصرة عثمان ودفع الغوغاء عنه فلأنه لم يأذن في ذلك وكان يتجافى عن الحرب وإراقة الدماء، حتى قال: من وضع السلاح من غلماني فهو حر. ومع ذلك فقد دفع عنه الحسنان ولم ينفع، وكان ما كان ولم يكن رضا من علي بذلك وإعانة عليه. ولهذا قال: والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات عليه. وتوقف في قبول البيعة إعظاماً لقتل عثمان وإنكاراً، وكذا طلحة والزبير. إلا أن من حضر من وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار أقسموا عليه وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمة وصيانة دار الهجرة، إذ قتلة عثمان قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة والفتك بأهلها. وكانوا جهلة لا سابقة لهم في الإسلام ولا علم لهم بأمر الدين ولا صحبة مع الرسول فقبل البيعة. وتوقفه عن قصاص قتلة عثمان إما لشوكتهم وكثرتهم وقوتهم وحرصهم بالخروج على من يطالبهم بدمه فاقتضى النظر الصائب تأخير الأمر احترازاً عن إثارة الفتنة، وإما لأنه رأى أنهم بغاة لما لهم من المنعة الظاهرة والتأويل الفاسد حيث استحلوا دمه بما أنكروا عليه من الأمور، وأن الباغي إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤاخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموالهم وسفك دمائهم، على ما هو رأي بعض المجتهدين.
One of them is protecting sound intellects from being polluted by vile beliefs that the tales of some Rawafid and their narrations cause them to fall into. The second of them is basing some juridical rulings concerning the chapter of rebels (bughat) upon it, since there are no texts to refer to in this regard. Hence Abu Hanifah said: 'Were it not for Ali, we would not have known the conduct (sirah) regarding the Khawarij; and the Prophet specifically taught Ali those rulings because he knew of his particular need for them, or others also knew them, but they did not need to explain and convey since they saw Ali's treatment in accordance with them without alteration.' So we say: As for Ali's hesitation regarding the pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr, it is interpreted as due to the grief and sorrow that befell him from the loss of the Messenger of God, so he did not have leisure for consideration and ijtihad. When he considered and the truth became manifest to him, he entered into what the community entered. As for his hesitation about supporting Uthman and repelling the rabble from him, it was because he (Uthman) did not permit that, and he (Ali) was averse to war and bloodshed, even saying: 'Whoever among my slaves lays down his weapon is free.' Yet despite that, al-Hasan and al-Husayn defended him but it did not avail; and what happened happened, and Ali was not pleased with that nor did he assist it. For this reason he said: 'By God, I did not kill Uthman nor did I incite (malat) against him.' And his hesitation in accepting the caliphate was out of veneration for Uthman's murder and disapproval—likewise Talhah and al-Zubayr. However, those present from the notables of the Emigrants and Helpers adjured him and implored him by God for the preservation of the rest of the community and the protection of the city of emigration (Madinah), since Uthman's killers intended to seize control of Madinah and massacre its people. They were ignorant ones with no prior standing in Islam, no knowledge of religious affairs, and no companionship with the Messenger, so he accepted the caliphate. And his hesitation about exacting retribution from Uthman's killers was either due to their strength, number, power, and eagerness to revolt against anyone who demanded his blood, so sound judgment necessitated postponing the matter to avoid stirring up sedition, or because he considered them rebels (bughat) due to their manifest power and corrupt interpretation (ta'wil), whereby they considered his blood lawful because of what they disapproved of in him; and that when a rebel submits to the imam of justice, he is not held liable for what he previously committed of destroying their property and shedding their blood, according to the view of some mujtahids.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of two benefits and a defense of Ali's actions during the early fitnah. The author begins by stating two benefits of discussing these historical disputes: first, to protect sound minds from being corrupted by the vile beliefs that the tales of some Rawafid (a derogatory term for certain Shia) cause; second, to derive some juridical rulings for the chapter of rebels (bughat), since there are no explicit texts to refer to. He supports this by citing Abu Hanifah, who said that without Ali we would not have known the proper conduct regarding the Khawarij. The author then proceeds with a detailed defense ('we say') covering several points. First, Ali's delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr is attributed to his grief over the Prophet's death, not to any dispute; once he considered the matter, he joined the community. Second, Ali's failure to actively defend Uthman is explained by Uthman not having authorized it and Ali's aversion to war. However, al-Hasan and al-Husayn did defend Uthman, and Ali explicitly denied killing or inciting against him. Third, Ali's hesitation to accept the caliphate was due to his veneration for Uthman's murder, but he was compelled by the leading Companions who feared the killers would take over Madinah. Fourth, his delay in punishing Uthman's killers is justified either by their strength (to avoid more fitnah) or because he considered them rebels with a corrupt interpretation, and according to some jurists, rebels who submit are not punished for prior acts. The passage thus systematically clears Ali of any blame.

Key Terms
الروافض
البغاة
الفتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Discussing the early fitnah is merely historical curiosity or leads to sectarian division.
Correction: The author shows it serves both doctrinal protection and legal derivation, making it a valuable scholarly pursuit.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one benefit of discussing the early fitnah?
A) To prove that Ali was the rightful caliph.
B) To protect sound intellects from vile beliefs.
C) To establish the superiority of the Companions.
D) To critique the Khawarij only.

Key Takeaway
Studying the early fitnah protects faith and provides legal precedents for dealing with rebels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed discussions of early Muslim conflicts as irrelevant to your faith today? How does this lesson challenge that attitude, and what practical benefit might you now seek from studying them?
?
The author warns that narrations from certain sources can pollute sound intellects. Are you careful about where you get your information on Islamic history? What steps will you take to protect your mind from vile beliefs?
?
Abu Hanifah said that without Ali we would not know the proper conduct toward rebels. In your own life, have you ever needed a prophetic example to handle a difficult situation? How can you actively learn from the Sahabah’s responses to conflict?

Defense of Ali's Actions During the Fitnah
Author defends Ali's decisions regarding Abu Bakr, Uthman, caliphate, and retribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly defense of Ali's decisions regarding the caliphate, Uthman's murder, and retribution.

Core Idea
Ali's controversial actions were justified by grief, necessity, and sound judgment.

Having stated the benefits of discussing fitnah, the author now addresses common criticisms of Ali's conduct. He systematically explains each of Ali's decisions that have been questioned, showing that they were not indications of disagreement or complicity but were based on valid reasons. This defense is essential for maintaining a balanced view of the early Islamic community.

Guided Path
1
Read the defense passage
Read the Arabic and translation starting from 'So we say'. Note the four main points: delay in pledging to Abu Bakr, hesitation to support Uthman, delay in accepting caliphate, and delay in retribution.
The passage is structured as a response ('So we say').
2
Analyze the first point: delay in pledging to Abu Bakr
The author attributes it to grief over the Prophet's death, not to rejection of Abu Bakr. Once Ali considered the matter, he joined the community.
This refutes claims that Ali opposed Abu Bakr.
3
Analyze the remaining three points
For Uthman: Ali did not intervene because Uthman did not authorize it, and Ali was averse to bloodshed. For the caliphate: he hesitated out of respect for Uthman's murder but was compelled by the Companions. For retribution: he delayed to avoid further fitnah or because he considered the killers rebels with corrupt interpretation.
Each explanation shows Ali's prudence and adherence to Islamic principles.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Notice how the author uses scholarly reasoning (ijtihad, ta'wil) to justify Ali's actions, highlighting that Ali's conduct was consistent with Islamic law and communal welfare.
This approach provides a framework for understanding other controversial events in Islamic history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما صون الأذهان السليمة عن التدنس بالعقائد الردية التي توقعها حكايات بعض الروافض ورواياتهم. وثانيها ابتناء بعض الأحكام الفقهية في باب البغاة عليها. إذ ليس في ذلك نصوص يرجع إليها، ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة (۱) لولا علي لم نكن نعرف السيرة في الخوارج وكان النبي خص عليا بتعليم تلك الأحكام لما علم من اختصاصه بالحاجة إليها أو علمها غيره أيضاً، لكنهم لم يحتاجوا إلى البيان والتبليغ لما رأوا من معاملة علي على وفقها من غير تغيير. فنقول: أما توقف علي في بيعة أبي بكر فيحمل على أنه لما أصابه من الكآبة والحزن بفقد رسول الله لم يتفرغ للنظر والاجتهاد. فلما نظر وظهر له الحق، دخل فيما دخل فيه الجماعة. وأما توقفه عن نصرة عثمان ودفع الغوغاء عنه فلأنه لم يأذن في ذلك وكان يتجافى عن الحرب وإراقة الدماء، حتى قال: من وضع السلاح من غلماني فهو حر. ومع ذلك فقد دفع عنه الحسنان ولم ينفع، وكان ما كان ولم يكن رضا من علي بذلك وإعانة عليه. ولهذا قال: والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات عليه. وتوقف في قبول البيعة إعظاماً لقتل عثمان وإنكاراً، وكذا طلحة والزبير. إلا أن من حضر من وجوه المهاجرين والأنصار أقسموا عليه وناشدوه الله في حفظ بقية الأمة وصيانة دار الهجرة، إذ قتلة عثمان قصدوا الاستيلاء على المدينة والفتك بأهلها. وكانوا جهلة لا سابقة لهم في الإسلام ولا علم لهم بأمر الدين ولا صحبة مع الرسول فقبل البيعة. وتوقفه عن قصاص قتلة عثمان إما لشوكتهم وكثرتهم وقوتهم وحرصهم بالخروج على من يطالبهم بدمه فاقتضى النظر الصائب تأخير الأمر احترازاً عن إثارة الفتنة، وإما لأنه رأى أنهم بغاة لما لهم من المنعة الظاهرة والتأويل الفاسد حيث استحلوا دمه بما أنكروا عليه من الأمور، وأن الباغي إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤاخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموالهم وسفك دمائهم، على ما هو رأي بعض المجتهدين.
One of them is protecting sound intellects from being polluted by vile beliefs that the tales of some Rawafid and their narrations cause them to fall into. The second of them is basing some juridical rulings concerning the chapter of rebels (bughat) upon it, since there are no texts to refer to in this regard. Hence Abu Hanifah said: 'Were it not for Ali, we would not have known the conduct (sirah) regarding the Khawarij; and the Prophet specifically taught Ali those rulings because he knew of his particular need for them, or others also knew them, but they did not need to explain and convey since they saw Ali's treatment in accordance with them without alteration.' So we say: As for Ali's hesitation regarding the pledge of allegiance to Abu Bakr, it is interpreted as due to the grief and sorrow that befell him from the loss of the Messenger of God, so he did not have leisure for consideration and ijtihad. When he considered and the truth became manifest to him, he entered into what the community entered. As for his hesitation about supporting Uthman and repelling the rabble from him, it was because he (Uthman) did not permit that, and he (Ali) was averse to war and bloodshed, even saying: 'Whoever among my slaves lays down his weapon is free.' Yet despite that, al-Hasan and al-Husayn defended him but it did not avail; and what happened happened, and Ali was not pleased with that nor did he assist it. For this reason he said: 'By God, I did not kill Uthman nor did I incite (malat) against him.' And his hesitation in accepting the caliphate was out of veneration for Uthman's murder and disapproval—likewise Talhah and al-Zubayr. However, those present from the notables of the Emigrants and Helpers adjured him and implored him by God for the preservation of the rest of the community and the protection of the city of emigration (Madinah), since Uthman's killers intended to seize control of Madinah and massacre its people. They were ignorant ones with no prior standing in Islam, no knowledge of religious affairs, and no companionship with the Messenger, so he accepted the caliphate. And his hesitation about exacting retribution from Uthman's killers was either due to their strength, number, power, and eagerness to revolt against anyone who demanded his blood, so sound judgment necessitated postponing the matter to avoid stirring up sedition, or because he considered them rebels (bughat) due to their manifest power and corrupt interpretation (ta'wil), whereby they considered his blood lawful because of what they disapproved of in him; and that when a rebel submits to the imam of justice, he is not held liable for what he previously committed of destroying their property and shedding their blood, according to the view of some mujtahids.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of two benefits and a defense of Ali's actions during the early fitnah. The author begins by stating two benefits of discussing these historical disputes: first, to protect sound minds from being corrupted by the vile beliefs that the tales of some Rawafid (a derogatory term for certain Shia) cause; second, to derive some juridical rulings for the chapter of rebels (bughat), since there are no explicit texts to refer to. He supports this by citing Abu Hanifah, who said that without Ali we would not have known the proper conduct regarding the Khawarij. The author then proceeds with a detailed defense ('we say') covering several points. First, Ali's delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr is attributed to his grief over the Prophet's death, not to any dispute; once he considered the matter, he joined the community. Second, Ali's failure to actively defend Uthman is explained by Uthman not having authorized it and Ali's aversion to war. However, al-Hasan and al-Husayn did defend Uthman, and Ali explicitly denied killing or inciting against him. Third, Ali's hesitation to accept the caliphate was due to his veneration for Uthman's murder, but he was compelled by the leading Companions who feared the killers would take over Madinah. Fourth, his delay in punishing Uthman's killers is justified either by their strength (to avoid more fitnah) or because he considered them rebels with a corrupt interpretation, and according to some jurists, rebels who submit are not punished for prior acts. The passage thus systematically clears Ali of any blame.

Key Terms
الصحابة
البيعة
الاجتهاد
البغاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's actions show he disagreed with Abu Bakr or was complicit in Uthman's death.
Correction: The author provides valid reasons: grief, lack of authorization, necessity, and sound judgment, clearing Ali of blame.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Ali delay in pledging allegiance to Abu Bakr?
A) Because he believed he was more entitled to the caliphate.
B) Due to grief and sorrow over the Prophet's death.
C) Because he was waiting for the Khawarij to support him.
D) Because he was unaware of the event.

Key Takeaway
Ali's actions during the fitnah were justified by circumstances and sound Islamic reasoning, refuting accusations of opposition or complicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali's hesitation to punish the killers was based on sound judgment to avoid greater evil. In your own life, when have you delayed a necessary action out of fear of causing more harm? How can you discern whether your delay is wisdom or procrastination?
?
The early Muslims rushed to judge Ali's actions, but this lesson shows the full context. Think of a time you judged someone harshly without knowing their circumstances. How can you train yourself to seek understanding before passing judgment?
?
Ali accepted the caliphate under pressure to protect the community from ignorant killers. Are there responsibilities you have avoided, and what would it cost others if you continue to avoid them? What is one step you can take today to step into a duty you have been neglecting?

Why Some Companions Did Not Join Ali's Battles
Author defends Companions who refrained from Ali's battles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that certain Companions refrained from supporting Ali in battle due to their own ijtihad and adherence to prophetic guidance, not because they rejected his legitimate imamate.

Core Idea
The Companions' restraint in war was pious ijtihad, not rebellion.

During the tumultuous events following the assassination of Uthman, several prominent Companions chose not to join Ali's military campaigns. This lesson examines the author's explanation for their decision. It clarifies that their stance was based on careful personal reasoning and teachings of the Prophet about avoiding fitnah, not on any denial of Ali's rightful leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's firm assertion that the Companions' refusal was not about rejecting Ali's imamate.
The passage defends figures like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas and others.
2
Understand the author's argument
The author distinguishes between refusing obedience and exercising ijtihad. Identify the key phrase 'due to their own ijtihad' and see how it frames the discussion.
3
Examine the cited hadith
The passage provides two hadith: one about breaking swords for a wooden one during fitnah, and another about the virtue of sitting during fitnah. Reflect on how these hadith support the Companions' choice to refrain.
These hadith emphasize avoiding inter-Muslim conflict.
4
Conclude the lesson's key point
The author concludes that they were not sinning. Consider how this defends the Companions' honor while affirming Ali's rightful position as caliph.
This reconciles the actions with the Sunni doctrine of the justice of all Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: وامتناع سعد وسعيد وغيرهما عن الخروج معه إلى الحروب كان لاجتهاد منهم، وترك إلزام منه، لا لنزاع في إمامته أو إباء عن طاعته. يعني أن امتناع جماعة من الصحابة كسعد بن أبي وقاص وسعيد بن زيد وأسامة بن زيد وعبد الله بن عمر وغيرهم عن نصرة علي والخروج معه إلى الحروب لم يكن عن نزاع منهم في إمامته ولا عن إباء عما وجب عليهم من طاعته. بل لأنه تركهم واختيارهم من غير إلزام على الخروج إلى الحروب، فاختاروا ذلك بناء على أحاديث رووها على ما قال محمد بن سلمة أن رسول الله عهد إلي إذا وقعت الفتنة أن أكسر سيفي وأتخذ مكانه سيفاً من خشب. وروى سعد بن أبي وقاص أنه قال صلى الله عليه وسلم سيكون بعدي فتنة القاعد فيها خير من القائم والقائم فيها خير من الماشي والماشي فيها خير من الساعي. وقال: قتال المسلم كفر وسبابه فسق ولا يحل للمسلم أن [يهجر] فوق ثلاثة أيام. فلم يأثموا قعدوا عن الحروب. قال: وأما في حرب الجمل
And he said: The refusal of Sa'd, Sa'id and others to go out with him to the wars was due to their own ijtihad, and his leaving them to choose without compulsion, not due to dispute over his imamate or refusal of his obedience. He means that the refusal of a group of the Companions, such as Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, Usamah ibn Zayd, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar and others, to support Ali and go out with him to the wars was not due to any dispute over his imamate nor due to refusal of the obedience due to him. Rather, it was because he left them and their choice without compelling them to go out to the wars. So they chose that based on hadith they narrated. As Muhammad ibn Salamah said: The Messenger of God entrusted to me that when fitnah occurs, I should break my sword and take in its place a sword of wood. And Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas narrated that he said: 'There will be after me a fitnah in which the one sitting is better than the one standing, the one standing better than the one walking, and the one walking better than the one striving.' And he said: 'Fighting a Muslim is unbelief, and reviling him is wickedness, and it is not lawful for a Muslim to [abandon] for more than three days.' So they were not sinning by staying away from the wars. He said: As for the Battle of the Camel...
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a defense of certain Companions (Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, Usamah ibn Zayd, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, and others) who did not join Ali's side in the battles during the fitnah. The author states that their refusal was an act of ijtihad (independent reasoning) on their part, and Ali did not compel them to go to war. It was not because they denied Ali's rightful caliphate or refused to obey him. Rather, Ali allowed them to choose, and they chose to stay away based on hadith they had received. The author cites two narrations: one from Muhammad ibn Salamah, who said the Prophet advised him to break his sword and use a wooden one during fitnah, signifying non-participation; and another from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas that the Prophet said that during a fitnah, the one who sits (i.e., refrains) is better than one who participates actively. The Prophet also said that fighting a Muslim is kufr (unbelief) and reviling him is fisq (sinfulness), and that Muslims should not abandon each other for more than three days. Based on these, they believed it was better to refrain from fighting to avoid greater sin and bloodshed. The author concludes that they were not sinning by staying away. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about the Battle of the Camel, possibly continuing to another point.

Key Takeaway

The Companions chose non-participation based on hadiths they knew. Are you as ready to act on your knowledge of the Prophet's teachings, even when it goes against what others expect? Commit to one specific teaching you will implement this week, even if it means sitting out a conflict.

The Companions’ Refraining from War: Ijtihad, Not Rebellion
Taftazani explains the ijtihad-based excuse of companions who did not join Ali in battle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certain companions chose not to fight alongside Ali, and how their decision was based on ijtihad and prophetic guidance rather than rejection of his leadership.

Core Idea
Refraining from fitna was a valid ijtihad, not a rejection of Ali’s imamate.

This passage addresses a sensitive historical issue: why did some notable companions like Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas not join Ali's army? Taftazani clarifies that their abstention was not due to any doubt about Ali's rightful caliphate, but rather a personal ijtihad informed by prophetic warnings about civil strife. Understanding this helps maintain respect for all companions while acknowledging their differing judgments in times of trial.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
Read the opening statement: the abstention was due to ijtihad and Ali's leaving them without compulsion, not dispute over imamate or disobedience.
The author immediately defends the companions' intentions.
2
Identify the companions and key terms
Note the names: Sa'd, Sa'id, Usama, Ibn Umar, etc. Focus on terms like امتناع, اجتهاد, إمامة, فتنة, إباء.
These terms recur in discussions of civil strife.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The companions relied on specific hadiths about avoiding fitna, e.g., breaking swords, sitting being better than standing, and prohibitions on fighting Muslims.
These hadiths show their precaution was based on prophetic guidance.
4
Reflect on the consequence: no sin
The passage concludes that they did not sin by refraining, emphasizing the legitimacy of their ijtihad.
This absolves them of blame.
5
Connect to broader lesson
Consider how this passage teaches us to judge historical figures by their intentions and evidence, not by hindsight.
It promotes a nuanced view of early Islamic history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: وامتناع سعد وسعيد وغيرهما عن الخروج معه إلى الحروب كان لاجتهاد منهم، وترك إلزام منه، لا لنزاع في إمامته أو إباء عن طاعته. يعني أن امتناع جماعة من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كسعد بن أبي وقاص وسعيد بن زيد وأسامة بن زيد وعبد الله بن عمر وغيرهم عن نصرة علي رضي الله عنه والخروج معه إلى الحروب لم يكن عن نزاع منهم في إمامته ولا عن إباء عما وجب عليهم من طاعته. بل لأنه تركهم واختيارهم من غير إلزام على الخروج إلى الحروب، فاختاروا ذلك بناء على أحاديث رووها على ما قال محمد بن سلمة أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عهد إلي إذا وقعت الفتنة أن أكسر سيفي، واتخذ مكانه سيفاً من خشب[؟]. وروى سعد بن أبي وقاص أنه قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: سيكون بعدي فتنة القاعد فيها خير من القائم والقائم فيها خير من الماشي، والماشي فيها خير من الساعي. وقال: قتال المسلم كفر وسبابه فسق، ولا يحل للمسلم أن يهجر فوق ثلاثة أيام. فلم تأثموا قعدوا عن الحروب.
And he said: The abstention of Sa'd and Sa'id and others from going out with him to the wars was due to their ijtihad (independent reasoning), and his leaving them without compulsion, not due to dispute about his imamate or unwillingness to obey him. That is, the abstention of a group of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) such as Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, Usama ibn Zayd, Abdullah ibn Umar, and others from supporting Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and going out with him to the wars was not due to a dispute from them about his imamate nor due to unwillingness regarding what was obligatory upon them of obedience to him. Rather, it was because he left them and their choice without compulsion to go out to the wars. So they chose that based on hadiths they narrated, as Muhammad ibn Salamah said: The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) entrusted to me that when tribulation occurs, I should break my sword and take in its place a sword of wood[?]. And Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas narrated that he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: There will be after me a tribulation in which the one who sits is better than the one who stands, and the one who stands is better than the one who walks, and the one who walks is better than the one who runs. And he said: Fighting a Muslim is disbelief, and insulting him is sin, and it is not permissible for a Muslim to abandon (his brother) for more than three days. So they did not sin by refraining from the wars.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the position of certain companions who did not participate in the battles alongside Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). The author (Taftazani) first states that the abstention of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and others was due to their own ijtihad, and that Ali left them free to choose without compulsion. It was not due to any dispute about Ali's rightful imamate or unwillingness to obey him. Then he elaborates: The companions who refrained from supporting and going out with Ali did so not because they rejected his imamate or refused the obligatory obedience to him. Rather, Ali left them to their own choice without forcing them to join the battles. They chose to abstain based on hadiths they had heard from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about avoiding fitna (tribulations). For example, Muhammad ibn Salamah reported that the Prophet commanded him in the event of fitna to break his sword and use a wooden one instead, symbolizing non-participation. Another hadith from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas indicates that during fitna, the one who remains seated is better than the one who stands, and so forth, encouraging avoidance. Additionally, a hadith states that fighting a Muslim is disbelief and insulting him is sin, and that it is not permissible to abandon a Muslim for more than three days. Therefore, these companions, by refraining from fighting, did not commit a sin.

Correction: The passage clarifies they acted on sincere ijtihad and prophetic guidance, not out of dispute or disobedience.

A) They disputed Ali's imamate
B) They were unwilling to obey him
C) They based their decision on hadiths about avoiding fitna
D) They were forced to stay away

Key Takeaway
The companions who refrained from fighting alongside Ali did so out of sincere ijtihad and adherence to prophetic guidance, not out of disobedience or denial of his imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between loyalty to a leader and your own conscience, whose voices do you listen to? Are you willing to 'break your sword' and suffer criticism, as the companions did, if you believe avoidance is better?
?
In your life, have you ever judged someone's inaction as disloyalty when they might have been acting on sincere ijtihad? How can you ensure you give others the benefit of the doubt as this passage teaches us about these companions?
?
Imagine you are in a situation where your teacher or imam asks you to participate in something that your heart hesitates about after seeking evidence. Would you have the courage to respectfully decline based on your own understanding, trusting that it might be accepted as ijtihad?

Ali's Correctness in the Three Battles and the Status of His Opponents
Taftazani affirms Ali's correctness and defines the status of his opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's position that Ali was correct in the battles of the Camel, Siffin, and the Khawarij, and that his opponents were rebels with a valid misinterpretation, not sinners or disbelievers.

Core Idea
Ali was right, his opponents were misguided but not disbelievers.

In this passage, Taftazani clarifies the Sunni position regarding the major battles during Ali's caliphate. He navigates between extreme views, affirming Ali's correctness while maintaining the status of his opponents as Muslims who erred due to misinterpretation. This lesson will unpack that position and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and technical terms.
2
Identify the positions Taftazani rejects
Find where Taftazani refutes the view that both parties were correct (Musawwiba) and the view that one party is correct without specification (some Mu'tazila). Understand why he rejects them.
3
Grasp the status of Ali's opponents
Taftazani labels them as 'bughat' (rebels) with a 'shubha' (misinterpretation). Understand the distinction between this and being sinners or disbelievers. Reflect on the evidence from Ali's own words.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding extreme sectarian views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما في حرب الجمل وحرب صفين وحرب الخوارج، فالمصيب علي، لما ثبت له من الإمامة وظهر من التفاوت، لا كلتا الطائفتين على ما هو رأي المصوبة ولا إحداهما من غير تعيين على ما هو رأي بعض المعتزلة والمخالفون بغاة لخروجهم على الإمام الحق لشبهة. لا فسقة أو كفرة على ما يزعم الشيعة جهلاً بالفرق بين المخالفة والمحاربة بالتأويل وبدونه. ولهذا نهى علي عن لعن أهل الشام، وقال: إخواننا بغوا علينا. وقد صح رجوع أصحاب الجمل. على أن منا من يقول: إن الحرب لم تقع عن عزيمة وإن قصد عائشة رضي الله عنها لم يكن إلا إصلاح ذات البين.
He said: As for the Battle of the Camel, the Battle of Siffin, and the Battle of the Khawarij, the one in the right is Ali, due to what is established of his imamate and the apparent difference (in status) – not both parties as is the opinion of the Musawwiba (those who consider both sides correct), nor one of them without specification as is the opinion of some of the Mu'tazila. And the opponents are rebels (bughat) due to their revolt against the rightful Imam based on a misconception (shubha), not sinners or disbelievers as the Shi'a claim out of ignorance of the difference between opposition and warfare with interpretation and without it. For this reason, Ali forbade cursing the people of Syria, and said: “Our brothers have revolted against us.” And it is authentically reported that some of the people of the Camel repented. Moreover, among us are those who say that the war did not occur out of resolve, and that the intention of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was only reconciliation.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the correct position regarding the three major battles during the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). The author asserts that Ali was in the right (al-musib) in all these battles: the Battle of the Camel, the Battle of Siffin, and the Battle of the Khawarij. This is because his imamate was established and there is a clear difference in status between him and his opponents. The author rejects two extreme views: first, the opinion of the Musawwiba (those who say both parties were correct), and second, the opinion of some Mu'tazila who say one of the two parties is correct without specifying which. Instead, he affirms that Ali alone was correct and that his opponents were bughat (rebels) who rose against the rightful imam based on a shubha (misinterpretation or doubt). They are not fasiq (sinners) or kafir (disbelievers) as the Shi'a claim, because the Shi'a fail to distinguish between opposing someone while having a valid interpretation (ta'wil) and opposing without it. Ali himself forbade cursing the people of Syria, calling them 'our brothers who have rebelled against us.' Additionally, it is authentically reported that some of the people of the Camel (the 'Aisha party) repented. Some scholars even say that the battle of the Camel was not intended by Aisha; her aim was only reconciliation.

Key Terms
المصيب
بغاة
شبهة
فسقة
كفرة
مخالفة
محاربة
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All participants in the battles (including Ali's opponents) are equally sinful, or the Khawarij are disbelievers.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Ali's opponents were rebels due to a misinterpretation (shubha), not sinners or disbelievers. They remained Muslims, as indicated by Ali calling them 'our brothers'.

Checkpoint
What is the status of Ali's opponents in the three battles according to Taftazani?
A) They are disbelievers
B) They are sinners
C) They are rebels (bughat) with a misinterpretation
D) They are equally correct as Ali

Key Takeaway
Taftazani teaches that Ali was right in the battles, and his opponents were Muslims who rebelled due to misinterpretation, not sinners or disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone as a kafir or fasiq simply because they opposed a leader you believed was right? How does Ali's refusal to curse his opponents challenge your own tendency to vilify those who disagree with you?
?
If Ali, the rightful Imam, called his opponents 'our brothers who rebelled against us,' what does that say about how you should treat Muslims who have wronged you or hold different political views?
?
Taftazani says the opponents were misguided but not sinners. Is there someone you have written off as completely wrong, whom you now need to reconsider as a brother or sister in faith? What specific step will you take this week to change that attitude?

The Three Groups Ali Fought: Nakithun, Mariqun, Qasitun
Taftazani classifies the three groups Ali fought based on prophetic hadith and describes each with historical detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identities and historical contexts of the three groups that Ali fought as foretold by the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
The Prophet foretold Ali would fight three types of deviators: oath-breakers, renegades, and the unjust.

This passage from Taftazani discusses a famous hadith in which the Prophet informs Ali that he will fight three groups of Muslims. The author then identifies each group with specific historical events from Ali's caliphate: the Battle of the Camel (nakithun), the Khawarij (mariqun), and the followers of Muawiya (qasitun). Understanding these groups is important for Sunni theology and historical perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the hadith frames the three groups and how Taftazani unpacks each one.
2
Identify each historical group
Match the three terms (nakithun, mariqun, qasitun) to the factions they represent: the people of the Camel, the Khawarij, and Muawiya's party.
Each group broke allegiance differently.
3
Analyze the reasons for conflict
For each group, note the specific cause of their opposition to Ali: breaking a pledge, rejecting arbitration, or deviating from the rightful caliph.
These are political and theological disputes among Muslims.
4
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why the Prophet's prophecy matters for Sunni theology: it legitimizes Ali's actions and shows that Muslims can oppose each other without necessarily leaving Islam.
The three groups are not labelled as disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاتل علي رضي الله عنه ثلاث فرق من المسلمين على ما قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: إنك تقاتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين. فالناكثون هم الذين نكثوا العهد والبيعة، وخرجوا إلى البصرة، مقدمهم طلحة والزبير رضي الله عنهما وقاتلوا عليا رضي الله عنه بعسكر مقدمهم عائشة رضي الله عنها في هودج على جمل أخذ بخطامه كعب بن مسعود فسمى ذلك الحرب حرب الجمل. والمارقون هم الذين نزعوا اليد عن طاعة علي رضي الله عنه بعدما بايعوه وتابعوه في حرب أهل الشام زعماً منهم أنه كفر حيث رضي بالتحكيم، وذلك أنه لما طالت محاربة علي رضي الله عنه ومعاوية بصفين واستمرت اتفق الفريقان على تحكيم أبي موسى الأشعري وعمرو بن العاص في أمر الخلافة، وعلي الرضا بما يريانه، فاجتمع الخوارج على بن وهب الراسبي وساروا إلى النهروان وسار إليهم علي رضي الله عنه بعسكره وكسرهم، وقتل الكثير منهم، وذلك حرب الخوارج وحرب النهروان. والقاسطون معاوية وأتباعه الذين اجتمعوا عليه وعدلوا عن طريق الحق الذي هو بيعة علي رضي الله عنه والدخول تحت طاعته، ذهاباً إلى أنه عبد الله.
Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) fought three groups of Muslims, as the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: "You will fight the breachers of oaths (al-nakithin), the renegades (al-mariqin), and the unjust (al-qasitin)." Al-nakithun are those who broke the covenant and the pledge, and went out to Basra, led by Talha and al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with them both), and they fought Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) with an army whose forefront was Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) in a howdah on a camel whose nose-rein was taken by Ka'b ibn Mas'ud – therefore that war was called the Battle of the Camel. Al-mariqun are those who withdrew their hand from obedience to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) after they had pledged allegiance to him and followed him in the war against the people of Syria, claiming that he had disbelieved because he accepted arbitration. That is, when the fighting between Ali and Muawiya at Siffin became prolonged and continued, the two parties agreed to arbitrate Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Amr ibn al-As concerning the matter of the caliphate, with Ali being content with what they would decide. So the Khawarij gathered around Ibn Wahb al-Rasibi and set out to al-Nahrawan. Ali advanced against them with his army, broke them, and killed many of them – that is the war of the Khawarij and the war of al-Nahrawan. Al-qasitun are Muawiya and his followers who gathered with him and deviated from the path of truth, which is the allegiance of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and entering under his obedience, going so far as to (claim) that he is Abdullah(?/).
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage classifies the three groups that Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) fought, based on a prophetic hadith. The Prophet told Ali that he would fight three groups: al-nakithun (the oath-breakers), al-mariqun (the ones who slip out or renegades), and al-qasitun (the unjust). The author then defines each group. First, the nakithun are those who broke their covenant and pledge of allegiance and marched to Basra, led by Talha and al-Zubayr. They fought Ali with an army that included Aisha riding a camel, hence the name "Battle of the Camel." Second, the mariqun are those who withdrew their obedience from Ali after having pledged allegiance to him and joined him in the war against the people of Syria (i.e., Muawiya). They accused Ali of disbelief because he agreed to arbitration between him and Muawiya. This refers to the Battle of Siffin, where after prolonged fighting, the two sides agreed to appoint two arbiters (Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Amr ibn al-As) to decide the caliphate issue, and Ali consented to accept their decision. A group that became known as the Khawarij rejected this, gathered under Ibn Wahb al-Rasibi, and moved to al-Nahrawan. Ali fought them, defeated them, and killed many. Third, the qasitun are Muawiya and his followers who united around him and deviated from the truth, which was recognizing Ali's caliphate and obeying him. The text ends with an unclear phrase indicating extreme claims.

Key Terms

المايقين
القاسطين
نكثوا العهد والبيعة
الخوارج
التحكيم
بغاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith about three groups applies only to the Khawarij.
Correction: The hadith explicitly includes the oath-breakers (Jamal) and the unjust (Muawiya) as well, not just the Khawarij.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, which groups did Ali fight?
A) Nakithun (oath-breakers), mariqun (renegades), and qasitun (unjust)
B) Mushrikun, munafiqun, and kafirun
C) Jamaliyyun, Siffiniyyun, and Khawarij
D) Talha, Zubayr, and Muawiya

Key Takeaway
Ali fought three groups of Muslims—oath-breakers (nakithun), renegades (mariqun), and the unjust (qasitun)—each representing a distinct form of rebellion against legitimate authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there promises you have made to Allah or to others that you have been tempted to break (like the nakithun)? What will you do this week to honor them?
?
When you disagree with a leader or a decision that seems unjust, do you react by withdrawing all support (like the mariqun) or do you seek constructive correction? How can you guard against rebellion?
?
Is there any area in your life where you have deviated from the truth (like the qasitun) due to personal bias or worldly desires? What step can you take today to realign with what is right?
