The Three Groups Ali Fought: Nakithun, Mariqun, Qasitun
Definition of the three groups Ali fought: nakithun, mariqun, qasitun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three groups that Imam Ali fought as foretold by the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the historical background of each conflict.

Core Idea
The Prophet foretold that Ali would fight three groups: covenant-breakers, renegades, and oppressors.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a famous hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed Ali that he would fight three groups of Muslims. The author defines each group and recounts the historical events that led to the battles of the Camel, Nahrawan, and Siffin. Understanding these groups helps contextualize early Islamic civil disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the three distinct groups mentioned and the battles associated with each.
2
Identify the nakithun (covenant-breakers)
Focus on the first group: those who broke their allegiance to Ali. Identify the key figures (Talha, Zubayr, Aisha) and the Battle of the Camel. Notice how the battle got its name.
The camel (jamal) gave the battle its name.
3
Identify the mariqun (renegades)
Understand the second group: the Kharijites who initially supported Ali but rebelled after he accepted arbitration. This led to the Battle of Nahrawan. Note their claim that Ali had disbelieved.
The term 'mariqun' is often translated as 'renegades' or 'those who slip out of the religion'.
4
Identify the qasitun (oppressors)
Learn about the third group: Muawiya and his followers who opposed Ali's caliphate and deviated from the truth. This led to the Battle of Siffin. Note the garbled part in the Arabic regarding their pretext.
The passage has an OCR issue regarding their claim about Uthman's killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاتل علي (رضي الله عنه) ثلاث فرق من المسلمين على ما قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم): إنك تقاتل الناكثين والمارقين والقاسطين. فالناكثون هم الذين نكثوا العهد والبيعة، وخرجوا إلى البصرة، مقدمهم طلحة والزبير (رضي الله عنهما) وقاتلوا عليا (رضي الله عنه) بعسكر مقدمهم عائشة رضي الله عنها في هودج على جمل أخذ بخطامه كعب بن مسعود فسمى ذلك الحرب حرب الجمل. والمارقون هم الذين نزعوا اليد عن طاعة علي (رضي الله عنه) بعدما بايعوه وتابعوه في حرب أهل الشام زعماً منهم أنه كفر حيث رضي بالتحكيم، وذلك أنه لما طالت محاربة علي (رضي الله عنه) ومعاوية بصفين واستمرت اتفق الفريقان على تحكيم أبي موسى الأشعري وعمرو بن العاص في أمر الخلافة، وعلي الرضا بما يريانه، فاجتمع الخوارج على ابن وهب الراسبي وساروا إلى النهروان وسار إليهم علي (رضي الله عنه) بعسكره وكسرهم، وقتل الكثير منهم، وذلك حرب الخوارج وحرب النهروان. والقاسطون معاوية وأتباعه الذين اجتمعوا عليه وعدلوا عن طريق الحق الذي هو بيعة علي (رضي الله عنه) والدخول تحت طاعته، ذهاباً إلى أنه [؟] الله قتلة [؟] مالاً على قتل عثمان رضي الله عنه حيث ترك معاونته، وجعل قتلته خواصه وبطانته، فاجتمع الفريقان بصفين وهي قرية خراب من قرى الروم على غلوة من الفرات، ودامت الحرب بينهم شهوراً، فسمي ذلك حرب صفين.
Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) fought three groups of Muslims, as the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'You will fight the breakers of covenant, the renegades, and the oppressors.' The breakers of covenant are those who broke the covenant and the pledge of allegiance, and went to Basra, led by Talha and al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with them both), and they fought Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) with an army led by Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) in a howdah on a camel whose rein was held by Ka'b ibn Mas'ud, so that war was called the Battle of the Camel. The renegades are those who withdrew their hand from obedience to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) after they had pledged allegiance and followed him in the war against the people of Syria, claiming that he had disbelieved because he accepted arbitration. This was when the battle between Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Muawiya at Siffin lasted long, and the two parties agreed to arbitrate Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Amr ibn al-As regarding the caliphate, with Ali accepting what they would decide. So the Kharijites gathered under Ibn Wahb al-Rasibi and marched to Nahrawan; Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) marched against them with his army, broke them, and killed many of them. That was the Battle of the Kharijites and the Battle of Nahrawan. As for the oppressors, they are Muawiya and his followers who gathered around him and deviated from the path of truth, which is the allegiance to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and submission to his authority, claiming that [؟] Allah [؟] wealth regarding the killing of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) because he abandoned supporting him and made his killers his special associates and close advisors. So the two parties met at Siffin, which is a ruined village of the Romans, a bowshot away from the Euphrates, and the war lasted between them for months, so that was called the Battle of Siffin.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage defines the three groups that Imam Ali fought, as mentioned in the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author first states that Ali fought three groups: the nakithun (breakers of covenant), the mariqun (renegades), and the qasitun (oppressors). He then explains each:

1. The nakithun: Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha who broke their allegiance to Ali, leading to the Battle of the Camel. The battle is named after Aisha's camel.

2. The mariqun: The Kharijites who initially supported Ali but later rebelled when he agreed to arbitration at Siffin, claiming he had disbelieved. They gathered under Ibn Wahb al-Rasibi and were defeated at Nahrawan.

3. The qasitun: Muawiya and his followers who deviated from the truth by opposing Ali's caliphate. Their pretext was Ali's alleged failure to seek retribution for Uthman's murder. This led to the Battle of Siffin.

The passage provides historical context for the early Islamic civil wars and identifies the key figures in each conflict.

Key Terms
ناكثون
مارقون
قاسطون
حرب الجمل
حرب صفين
حرب النهروان
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the groups Ali fought were non-Muslims or apostates.
Correction: The passage explicitly says Ali fought 'three groups of Muslims' (ثلاث فرق من المسلمين). The hadith refers to internal conflicts, not against non-believers.

Checkpoint
Which battle is associated with the nakithun (covenant-breakers)?
A) Battle of the Camel
B) Battle of Siffin
C) Battle of Nahrawan
D) Battle of Badr

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the three groups is a prophecy fulfilled, and each group represents a deviation from the right path, but they were still considered Muslims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever broken a promise you made to Allah or to a fellow Muslim, like the nakithun broke their bay'a? What justified it at the time, and what does this passage say about the weight of a covenant?
?
When you see a disagreement among Muslims, do you tend to side immediately with one party like the Kharijites did? How can you ensure you don't walk away from the truth like the mariqun?
?
Are there areas in your life where you know the truth—like the qasitun knew Ali was the caliph—but you choose a path of convenience instead? What one step can you take today to abandon that deviation and submit to Allah's command?

The Consensus on Ali's Correctness
Sunni consensus that Ali was correct in all conflicts due to legitimate imamate and superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus that Ali (r) was correct in all the conflicts during his caliphate, based on his legitimate imamate and clear superiority.

Core Idea
Ali was the rightful imam, and the truth was with him in every conflict.

Before examining the status of those who opposed Ali, the Sunni tradition firmly establishes that Ali was in the right. This lesson presents the core consensus and the evidence that led scholars to this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the consensus
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the strong language of agreement ('what the people of truth agreed upon').
2
Identify the reasons for Ali's correctness
The passage gives four reasons: his imamate by pledge, his clear superiority over opponents, multiple reports that truth is with him, and agreement even from enemies that he is best of his time.
Why do you think the author mentions agreement from enemies? This shows the strength of the consensus.
3
Reflect on the significance of this consensus
This consensus is foundational for Sunni identity. It establishes that Ali was right and any later evaluation of the conflicts starts from this premise.
Consider how this position differs from other sectarian views that may condemn both sides or absolve both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي اتفق عليه أهل الحق أن المصيب في جميع ذلك علي (رضي الله عنه) لما ثبت من إمامته ببيعة أهل الحل والعقد وظهر من تفاوت إما بينه وبين المخالفين سيما معاوية وأحزابه، وتكاثر من الأخبار في كون الحق معه، وما وقع عليه الاتفاق حتى من الأعداء إلى أنه أفضل زمانه وأنه لا أحق بالإمامة منه. والمخالفون بغاة لخروجهم على الإمام الحق بشبهة هي تركه القصاص من عثمان (رضي الله عنه) ولقوله الا الله العمار: «تقتلك الفئة الباغية» وقد قتل يوم صفين على يد أهل الشام. ولقول علي رضي الله عنه: إخواننا بغوا علينا، وليسوا كفاراً ولا فسقة ولا ظلمة لما لهم من التأويل. وإن كان باطلاً فغاية الأمر أنهم أخطأوا في الاجتهاد. وذلك لا يوجب التفسيق، فضلاً عن التكفير. ولهذا منع علي (رضي الله عنه) أصحابه من لعن أهل الشام، وقال: إخواننا بغوا علينا. كيف وقد صح ندم طلحة والزبير (رضي الله عنهما) وانصراف الزبير عن الحرب، واشتهر ندم عائشة رضي الله عنها. والمحقون من أصحابنا على أن حرب الجمل كانت فلتة من غير قصد من الفريقين، بل كانت تهييجاً من قتلة عثمان (رضي الله عنه) حيث صاروا فرقتين، واختلطوا بالعسكرين، وأقاموا الحرب خوفاً من القصاص، وقصد عائشة رضي الله عنها لم يكن إلا إصلاح الطائفتين وتسكين الفتنة، فوقعت في الحرب.
And what the people of truth agreed upon is that the one correct in all that is Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), because his imamate was established by the pledge of the people of loosening and binding, and there appeared a disparity between him and the opponents, especially Muawiya and his parties, and numerous reports concur that the truth is with him, and there is agreement even among enemies that he is the best of his time and that no one is more entitled to the imamate than him. The opponents are rebels (bughah) because they revolted against the true imam based on a pretext, namely his neglect of retaliation for Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him), and because of his (the Prophet's) saying: 'A rebellious group will kill you,' and he was killed on the day of Siffin at the hands of the people of Syria, and because of the saying of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' They are not disbelievers nor sinners nor oppressors because they have an interpretation (ta'wil). Even if that interpretation is false, at most they made a mistake in ijtihad. That does not necessitate declaring them sinful (tafsiq), let alone declaring them disbelievers (takfir). For this reason, Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) forbade his companions from cursing the people of Syria and said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' Moreover, it is established that Talha and al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with them both) regretted and al-Zubayr withdrew from the war, and the regret of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) is well-known. The correct ones among our companions hold that the Battle of the Camel was an accident without intention from either party, but rather it was instigated by the killers of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) who split into two groups, mixed with the two armies, and started the war out of fear of retaliation. The intention of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was only to reconcile the two groups and calm the sedition, but she ended up in the war.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni orthodoxy) on the conflicts between Ali and his opponents. The author states the consensus that Ali was correct in all these conflicts because his imamate was established through the pledge of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-aqd), and there is clear evidence of his superiority in knowledge and virtue. The opponents (including Muawiya and his party) are classified as bughah (rebels) because they fought against the legitimate imam, but they acted on a pretext (shubha) — namely Ali's failure to execute qisas (retaliation) for Uthman's murder. The author supports this with the hadith that a rebellious group (fia baghiya) would kill Ammar ibn Yasir, and with Ali's own statement that they are 'our brothers who have rebelled against us.' Because they have an interpretation (ta'wil), even if it is invalid, they are not considered sinners (fussaq) or disbelievers (kuffar); at worst they made an error in ijtihad, which does not entail tafsiq or takfir. Thus Ali forbade cursing them. The passage also notes the regrets of Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha, and cites the opinion of some scholars that the Battle of the Camel was accidental, instigated by the killers of Uthman who infiltrated both armies.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
إمامة
بيعة أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Sunni consensus that Ali was correct?
A) He was chosen by the Prophet directly.
B) His imamate was established by the pledge of the people of loosening and binding.
C) He won all battles.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni tradition holds that Ali was the rightful imam and was correct in all conflicts, based on his legitimate imamate and his clear superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept in your heart that Ali was correct in every conflict, even when it means that those who opposed him were wrong? Are you willing to submit to this consensus even if it challenges your personal sympathies? Ask Allah to purify your heart from partisanship.
?
The passage says that even the enemies of Ali admitted his superiority. Is there someone you disagree with or dislike whose virtue you privately acknowledge? What would it take for you to speak that truth openly, as a testimony to fairness?
?
If you were in a position like Ali’s, with the truth on your side but facing strong opposition, would your faith remain firm? Reflect on your reliance on proofs—are they enough to sustain you, or do you need worldly support? Commit today to strengthening your trust in the truth even when it is unpopular.

The Status of Ali's Opponents: Rebels with Interpretation
Classification of opponents as bughah with ta'wil, not sinners or disbelievers, and the accidental nature of the Battle of Camel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni scholars consider Ali's opponents as rebels (bughah) with a valid interpretation (ta'wil), and why they are not deemed sinners or disbelievers.

Core Idea
Opposition to a legitimate imam does not automatically mean sin or disbelief if based on a valid interpretation.

Having established that Ali was correct, the Sunni tradition now addresses the status of those who fought him. This lesson explains how they are classified as rebels acting on a pretext, and why Ali himself treated them as brothers, not enemies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the opponents' status
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the key terms: bughah (rebels), shubha (pretext), ta'wil (interpretation), tafsiq (declaring sinful), takfir (declaring disbelief).
2
Understand the basis for calling them bughah
The passage gives three reasons: (a) they revolted against the true imam over a pretext (Ali's delay in qisas for Uthman), (b) the hadith about Ammar, and (c) Ali's own statement 'our brothers have rebelled against us'.
A pretext (shubha) means they had some grounds, even if mistaken.
3
Learn the ruling: not kuffar or fussaq
Because they had a ta'wil (interpretation), even if false, they are not sinful or oppressive. At worst they made an error in ijtihad. This does not justify tafsiq, let alone takfir.
The concept of ijtihad error is crucial: sincere but wrong reasoning does not make one a sinner.
4
Note the practical implications and the Battle of Camel
Ali forbade cursing them. Also, the regrets of Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha show their good intentions. The Battle of Camel was accidental, instigated by others.
This reinforces the idea that the fitna was not a simple black-and-white conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي اتفق عليه أهل الحق أن المصيب في جميع ذلك علي (رضي الله عنه) لما ثبت من إمامته ببيعة أهل الحل والعقد وظهر من تفاوت إما بينه وبين المخالفين سيما معاوية وأحزابه، وتكاثر من الأخبار في كون الحق معه، وما وقع عليه الاتفاق حتى من الأعداء إلى أنه أفضل زمانه وأنه لا أحق بالإمامة منه. والمخالفون بغاة لخروجهم على الإمام الحق بشبهة هي تركه القصاص من عثمان (رضي الله عنه) ولقوله الا الله العمار: «تقتلك الفئة الباغية» وقد قتل يوم صفين على يد أهل الشام. ولقول علي رضي الله عنه: إخواننا بغوا علينا، وليسوا كفاراً ولا فسقة ولا ظلمة لما لهم من التأويل. وإن كان باطلاً فغاية الأمر أنهم أخطأوا في الاجتهاد. وذلك لا يوجب التفسيق، فضلاً عن التكفير. ولهذا منع علي (رضي الله عنه) أصحابه من لعن أهل الشام، وقال: إخواننا بغوا علينا. كيف وقد صح ندم طلحة والزبير (رضي الله عنهما) وانصراف الزبير عن الحرب، واشتهر ندم عائشة رضي الله عنها. والمحقون من أصحابنا على أن حرب الجمل كانت فلتة من غير قصد من الفريقين، بل كانت تهييجاً من قتلة عثمان (رضي الله عنه) حيث صاروا فرقتين، واختلطوا بالعسكرين، وأقاموا الحرب خوفاً من القصاص، وقصد عائشة رضي الله عنها لم يكن إلا إصلاح الطائفتين وتسكين الفتنة، فوقعت في الحرب.
And what the people of truth agreed upon is that the one correct in all that is Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), because his imamate was established by the pledge of the people of loosening and binding, and there appeared a disparity between him and the opponents, especially Muawiya and his parties, and numerous reports concur that the truth is with him, and there is agreement even among enemies that he is the best of his time and that no one is more entitled to the imamate than him. The opponents are rebels (bughah) because they revolted against the true imam based on a pretext, namely his neglect of retaliation for Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him), and because of his (the Prophet's) saying: 'A rebellious group will kill you,' and he was killed on the day of Siffin at the hands of the people of Syria, and because of the saying of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' They are not disbelievers nor sinners nor oppressors because they have an interpretation (ta'wil). Even if that interpretation is false, at most they made a mistake in ijtihad. That does not necessitate declaring them sinful (tafsiq), let alone declaring them disbelievers (takfir). For this reason, Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) forbade his companions from cursing the people of Syria and said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' Moreover, it is established that Talha and al-Zubayr (may Allah be pleased with them both) regretted and al-Zubayr withdrew from the war, and the regret of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) is well-known. The correct ones among our companions hold that the Battle of the Camel was an accident without intention from either party, but rather it was instigated by the killers of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) who split into two groups, mixed with the two armies, and started the war out of fear of retaliation. The intention of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was only to reconcile the two groups and calm the sedition, but she ended up in the war.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni orthodoxy) on the conflicts between Ali and his opponents. The author states the consensus that Ali was correct in all these conflicts because his imamate was established through the pledge of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-aqd), and there is clear evidence of his superiority in knowledge and virtue. The opponents (including Muawiya and his party) are classified as bughah (rebels) because they fought against the legitimate imam, but they acted on a pretext (shubha) — namely Ali's failure to execute qisas (retaliation) for Uthman's murder. The author supports this with the hadith that a rebellious group (fia baghiya) would kill Ammar ibn Yasir, and with Ali's own statement that they are 'our brothers who have rebelled against us.' Because they have an interpretation (ta'wil), even if it is invalid, they are not considered sinners (fussaq) or disbelievers (kuffar); at worst they made an error in ijtihad, which does not entail tafsiq or takfir. Thus Ali forbade cursing them. The passage also notes the regrets of Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha, and cites the opinion of some scholars that the Battle of the Camel was accidental, instigated by the killers of Uthman who infiltrated both armies.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fighting against a legitimate imam automatically makes one a disbeliever or sinner.
Correction: The passage clarifies that if the opponents have a valid interpretation (ta'wil), even if mistaken, they are not sinful. At worst, they made an error in ijtihad, which does not entail tafsiq or takfir.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for not declaring Ali's opponents as sinners (fussaq)?
A) They repented after the battle.
B) They had a valid interpretation (ta'wil), even if mistaken.
C) They were forced to fight.
D) They did not actually fight Ali.

Key Takeaway
Opponents of Ali were rebels with a valid interpretation, thus not guilty of sin or disbelief, and the Battle of Camel was accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever condemned someone in your heart without considering that they might have a valid interpretation or sincere motive, even if mistaken? How will you apply Ali's example of refraining from cursing and instead calling them 'brothers'?
?
When you hear about a conflict among Muslims today, do you immediately take sides and assume ill intent of the other side? What would it cost you to pause and remember that even the Companions had disagreements that were accidental and excused?
?
If you were in a situation where your own good intention led to unintended harm (like Aisha's desire for reconciliation that ended in war), would you have the humility to admit your mistake and seek forgiveness? What is holding you back from that humility right now?

Refuting the Shi'a Claim on Ali's Opponents
Refutation of the Shi'a claim that Ali's opponents are disbelievers or sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Shi'a view that all who opposed Ali are disbelievers or sinners is rejected, and the key distinction between opposition with ta'wil/ijtihad and without.

Core Idea
Not all who oppose a rightful imam are disbelievers; ta'wil and ijtihad can excuse.

The Shi'a held that those who fought or opposed Ali ibn Abi Talib (RA) were disbelievers or sinners. This passage from a classical Sunni theological text challenges that view, arguing that it fails to account for legitimate interpretation (ta'wil) and ijtihad. The author also notes a separate case regarding the Kharijites. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's strong dismissal of the Shi'a position.
The Arabic includes the phrase 'من اجتراءاتهم وجهالاتهم' indicating severity.
2
Identify the Shi'a claim and evidence
The Shi'a claim that those who fought Ali are disbelievers and opponents are sinners. Their evidence is the hadith 'Warring against you is warring against me' and the principle that abandoning obligation is sin.
3
Understand the refutation
The author rejects this because the Shi'a failed to distinguish between opposition that arises from legitimate interpretation (ta'wil) and ijtihad, and opposition without such basis. This is the core of the argument.
Ta'wil means interpretation, often of ambiguous texts.
4
Note the Kharijite concession
The author adds that if we were to declare Kharijites disbelievers for their takfir of Ali, it would not be far-fetched, but that is a separate issue.
This shows the author is not absolving all opponents, only those with valid ta'wil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذهب إليه الشيعة من أن محاربي علي كفرة، ومخالفوه فسقة تمسكاً بقوله (الله) حربك يا علي حربي. وبأن الطاعة واجبة، وترك الواجب فسق، فمن اجتراءاتهم وجهالاتهم حيث لم يفرقوا بين ما يكون بتأويل واجتهاد وبين ما لا يكون. نعم: لو قلنا بكفر الخوارج بناء على تكفيرهم علياً (رضي الله عنه): لم يبعد، لكنه بحث آخر.
And what the Shi'a have gone to, that those who fought Ali are disbelievers and those who opposed him are sinners, clinging to his saying: 'Warring against you, O Ali, is warring against me,' and that obedience is obligatory and abandoning the obligatory is sin, is among their audacities and ignorances, because they did not distinguish between what is done with interpretation and ijtihad and what is not. Yes, if we were to declare the Kharijites disbelievers on the basis of their declaring Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) a disbeliever, it would not be far-fetched, but that is another discussion.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shi'a position that all who fought or opposed Ali are disbelievers or sinners. The author argues that the Shi'a base their claim on the hadith 'Warring against you, O Ali, is warring against me' and on the principle that obedience is obligatory while its abandonment is sin. However, the author dismisses this as audacity and ignorance because they fail to distinguish between opposition that arises from interpretation (ta'wil) and ijtihad (which can excuse) and opposition without such basis. The author then makes an important concession: if we were to declare the Kharijites disbelievers because they declared Ali a disbeliever (takfir), that would not be far-fetched, but that is a separate issue. This shows that the Shi'a blanket condemnation is rejected, while the Kharijites' case is different due to their own act of takfir.

Key Terms
شيعة
كفرة
فسقة
تأويل
اجتهاد
تكفير
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all historical opponents of Ali are equally condemned in Sunni theology.
Correction: The author distinguishes between those who opposed Ali based on ta'wil and ijtihad (like Muawiya) and those who themselves committed takfir (like Kharijites). The former are not automatically disbelievers or sinners.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Shi'a claim that all who fought Ali are disbelievers?
A) Because the hadith about warring is weak
B) Because the opponents repented
C) Because the opponents had a ta'wil and ijtihad that excuses them
D) Because Ali forgave them

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a claim that all who opposed Ali are disbelievers or sinners is rejected because it ignores the possibility of legitimate interpretation (ta'wil) and ijtihad; however, those who themselves commit takfir are a separate case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever condemned someone's actions without considering whether they might have a valid interpretation (ta'wil) that you are unaware of? How can you train yourself to pause and ask, 'Could this person be excused by ijtihad?' before judging them?
?
The Shi'a clung to a hadith to justify their blanket condemnation. Are there any hadiths or principles you have used to criticize fellow Muslims without giving them the benefit of the doubt? What would it take for you to apply the lesson's distinction between opposition with ta'wil and without?
?
The author separates those who commit takfir as a special case. Is there someone you have considered beyond the pale of Islam because of their actions? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own readiness to pronounce takfir and to reserve judgment for the 'separate discussion'?
